اور رہا متن کا مسئلہ یعنی جب غلام نے اپنی ذات کو اپنے واسطے خریدنے کا وکیل کیا تو اس صورت میں وکیل پر یہ بیان کرنا ضروری ہے کہ میں نے اس غلام کو خود اس کی ذات کے واسطے خریدا ہے۔ کیونکہ اس مسئلہ میں بائع یعنی مولیٰ کے حق میں دونوں عقد مختلف ہیں۔ اسلئے اس مسئلہ میں اگر وکیل نے شراء (خریداری) کو اپنے مؤکل ( غلام ) کی طرف منسوب کیا تو یہ مولی (بائع ) کے حق میں اعتناق علی مال ہوگا اور مولی ( بائع ) کیلئے ولاء ثابت ہوگی ۔ اور وکیل پرشمن (مال) وغیرہ کا مطالبہ نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ اس صورت میں وکیل سفیر محض ہوتا ہے۔ حقوق عقد اس کی طرف راج نہیں ہوتے ہیں اور اگر وکیل نے عقد شراء کو اپنے مؤکل کی طرف منسوب نہ کیا تو یہ عقد بائع (مولی) کے حق میں مبیع ہو گا ۔ یعنی مولی غلام کو فروخت کرنے والا ہوگا اور وکیل اپنے واسطے خریدنے والا ہوگا۔ اور وکیل پر شمن و غیرہ کا مطالبہ واجب ہوگا اور کبھی بولی اتاق علی مال کو پسند نہیں کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ اس معاوضہ محضہ کو پسند کرتا ہے جس میں مطالبہ ثمن وکیل سے ہوتا ہے۔ اور اعتاق علی مال کو نا پسند کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اعتناق کی وجہ سے اگر چہ مولیٰ کیلئے ولاء ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ مگر مولی عتاقہ چونکہ اپنے آزاد شده غلام کا عاقلہ بھی ہے۔ اسلئے اگر اس آزاد کردہ غلام نے کوئی جنایت کی تو اسکا تاوان وغیرہ بھی مولیٰ پر واجب ہوتا ہے۔ پس جب مولی بسا اوقات اعتناق علی مال کو پسند نہیں کرتا اور معاوضہ کو پسند کرتا ہے تو وکیل پر واجب ہے کہ وہ اس صورت میں یہ وضاحت کر دے کہ میں نے اس غلام کو خود اس غلام کے واسطے خریدا ہے تا کہ مولی سوچ سمجھ کر جواب دے۔ چنانچہ اگر وکیل نے یہ وضاحت نہ کی تو یہ خریداری مؤکل یعنی غلام کے واسطے نہ ہوگی۔ بلکہ خود وکیل کے واسطے ہو گی ۔
And as for the issue of the text (matn): when a slave appoints an agent to buy his own self for himself, then in that case it is necessary for the agent to state: 'I bought this slave for his own self.' Because in this issue, from the perspective of the seller, i.e., the master, the two contracts are different. Therefore, in this issue, if the agent attributes the purchase to his principal (the slave), then this becomes from the master's perspective an emancipation in exchange for property (i'taq 'ala mal), and the master (the seller) acquires clientage (wala'), and the agent will not be demanded for the price etc., because it has passed earlier that in this case the agent is merely a messenger (safir); the rights of the contract do not revert to him. But if the agent does not attribute the purchase contract to his principal, then this contract becomes from the seller's (master's) perspective a sale; i.e., the master is the seller and the agent is the buyer for himself, and the agent will be demanded for the price etc. And sometimes the master does not like emancipation in exchange for property; rather, he likes a pure exchange in which the price is demanded from the agent. The reason for disliking emancipation in exchange for property is that although due to emancipation the master acquires clientage, the master is also the 'aqila (responsible for blood money) of his freed slave. So if the freed slave commits a crime, its compensation etc. becomes obligatory upon the master. So when the master sometimes does not like emancipation in exchange for property and likes the exchange, then it is obligatory upon the agent to clarify in this case that 'I bought this slave for the slave himself' so that the master considers and responds accordingly. So if the agent does not clarify, then this purchase is not for the principal (the slave); rather, it is for the agent himself.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding a specific scenario where a slave appoints an agent to buy the slave himself from his master. The key point is that the agent must explicitly state that he is buying the slave for the slave's own sake. The reason is that from the master's perspective, the transaction could be either a sale or an emancipation in exchange for property (i'taq 'ala mal). These have different consequences: if it is emancipation, the master gets clientage (wala') but also becomes the 'aqila for the freed slave's crimes, which may be burdensome. The master may prefer a simple sale. Therefore, the agent must clarify so the master can make an informed decision. If the agent fails to clarify, the purchase is for the agent himself, not the slave. This ruling emphasizes the importance of protecting the master's choice and ensuring transparency in agency contracts.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakil): The person appointed to act on behalf of another in a legal transaction.
اعتاق علی مال (i'taq 'ala mal): Emancipation of a slave in exchange for property paid by the slave to the master, considered a sale of freedom.
ولاء (wala'): The legal bond of clientage between a freed slave and the emancipator, entailing inheritance rights and responsibility for blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must an agent appointed by a slave to buy the slave himself clarify that the purchase is for the slave?
A) Because the agent is acting as a messenger and has no authority.
B) To allow the master to choose between sale and emancipation, which have different consequences.
C) Because the slave's earnings belong to the master.
D) To avoid confusion about the price.

Key Takeaway
An agent buying a slave for the slave himself must clarify the purchase; failure makes the agent the buyer and liable for the price.

Slave Acting as Agent and the Obligation of a Second Payment
Ruling with proof using qiyas and intra-Hanafi commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave can act as agent for another in buying himself and apply the qiyas rule to determine when a second payment is wajib.

Core Idea
A slave's earnings belong to the master, so paying with them is like paying with nothing, making a second payment wajib.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a scenario where a free person instructs a slave to buy himself from the master. The slave can validly act as agent because he is a stranger to his own value. However, if the slave uses his earnings to pay the price, that payment is ineffective since those earnings already belong to the master. Through qiyas, the Hanafi school concludes that a second payment equal to the price is wajib. The passage also explores an intra-Hanafi discussion about whether this obligation holds when the slave buys himself for his own emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the legal act: a free person instructs a slave to buy himself from the master. The ruling: the slave becomes the property of the free person through a valid sale and agency.
The slave acts as an agent (wakil) for the free person.
2
Analyze the basis of agency
Understand why the slave can be an agent: he is a stranger (ajnabi) to his own value (maliyyah) because his value is not his property. Thus, he can buy himself for another.
This is a Hanafi-specific ruling; other schools may differ.
3
Examine the payment issue
If the slave uses his own earnings to pay the master, that payment is invalid because the slave's earnings belong to the master from the start. Hence, no real transfer of consideration occurs.
The master already owned that money.
4
Apply the qiyas reasoning
The original case (asl): paying with the master's own property is invalid and requires a substitute. The new case (far'): the slave paying with his earnings is analogous. The illa (effective cause) is that the money belongs to the master, so it cannot serve as a substitute for emancipation.
The second payment is wajib to fulfill the substitute.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi discussion
When the slave buys himself for his own emancipation (shira' al-'abd nafsahu), commentators like Qadhi Khan and Sahib al-Inayah confirm that a second payment is still wajib, as the transaction is an emancipation with substitute, not gratuitous.
This is derived from the designation in al-Hidayah.

A free man said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' The slave said to his master: 'Myself for so-and-so, from my hand, for such a price.' The master did so. Then the slave is for the principal (the free man). And whoever says to a slave: 'Buy yourself for me from your master,' and he says to his master: 'Myself for so-and-so for such a price,' and he does it, then it is for the commander because the slave can act as an agent for another in buying himself, since he is a stranger to his own value (maliyyah), and the sale is directed upon him insofar as he is property; except that his value is in his hand such that the seller does not have the right to detain after sale to collect the price. So if he attributes it to the commander, his action is valid according to our school, and the contract occurs for the commander. Translation: And if a free man said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' Then the slave said to his master: 'My self for so-and-so, from my hand, for such a price.' So the master did it, then the slave is for the principal. Because the slave can be an agent for another in buying himself, as he is a stranger to his own value. And the sale is upon him... The book of agency. Ashraf al-Bidayah vol. - And his earnings belong to the master, and all of the slave's earnings belong to the master. Therefore, this one thousand rupees also belongs to the master. And the proof of the third part, i.e., the proof that a second thousand rupees is obligatory upon the buyer (agent), is that the buyer is liable for the price, and the one thousand rupees that the buyer gave to the master, since it is his slave's earnings, and the slave's earnings are the master's property, therefore this one thousand rupees does not count as the slave's price, and paying it as the price is not valid. So when the buyer is liable for the price, and the one thousand rupees he gave to the master is not valid as payment of the price, then a second one thousand rupees similar to it is obligatory upon the buyer as the slave's price. The authors of Fath al-Qadir, Inayah, and Kifayah said that the obligation of the second one thousand upon the buyer is clear when the purchase is for the buyer. But when the purchase is for the slave himself, such that the slave becomes free, then is a second one thousand still obligatory upon the slave? In al-Jami' al-Saghir, Qadhi Khan said: Although this is not mentioned in the book, it is appropriate that a second one thousand is obligatory upon the slave for the master, because the one thousand that the slave gave to his agent, and the agent gave to the master, that was the slave's earnings, so it is already the master's property. Hence it cannot be a substitute for emancipation. And when it cannot be a substitute, a second one thousand which is a substitute for emancipation is necessarily obligatory. Sahib al-Inayah responded that the text of al-Hidayah indicates that a second one thousand is obligatory upon the slave, in that the author of al-Hidayah said that the purchase of the slave himself (shira' al-'abd nafsahu) is accepting emancipation in exchange for a substitute. So if a second one thousand is not made obligatory, then this would be emancipation without substitute, whereas it has been designated as emancipation with substitute. So the author's designation indicates obligation. In contrast, if someone other than the slave appoints an agent to buy a slave (e.g., Wasif appoints Shahid to buy a slave from Arif for one thousand), then it is not necessary for the agent to state at the time of purchase that he bought it for his principal, because whether he says it or not, the purchase contract occurs for the principal. Both contracts (the one attributed to himself and the one attributed to the principal) are the same from the seller's perspective; both are sales, and the demand is directed to the agent. So when both cases are identical, clarification is not necessary.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses two related scenarios. First, a free person instructs a slave to buy himself from the master. The slave can act as agent for the free person because he is a stranger to his own value (maliyyah). The purchase is valid and the slave becomes the property of the free person. Second, the passage discusses payment complications: if the slave uses his own earnings (which actually belong to the master) to pay the price, that payment is ineffective because the money was already the master's. Therefore, a second payment equal to the price is due. Scholars like Qadhi Khan and Sahib al-Inayah confirm this, deducing from al-Hidayah that the transaction is an emancipation in exchange for a substitute, not a gratuitous emancipation. Finally, the passage contrasts this with ordinary agency for buying a slave from another free person, where clarification is unnecessary because the contract is always a sale from the seller's perspective and the agent is always liable.

Key Terms
شراء العبد نفسه (shira' al-'abd nafsahu): The slave purchasing himself from his master, resulting in his freedom in exchange for a substitute (badal).
اعتناق علی مال (i'taq 'ala mal): Emancipation in exchange for property paid by the slave.
وکیل (wakil): Agent; the slave acts as agent for the free person in buying himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a free person instructs a slave to buy himself, and the slave uses his own earnings to pay the master, what is the ruling?
A) The slave becomes free and no further payment is needed.
B) The slave must pay a second thousand because the first payment was invalid.
C) The master must return the money to the slave.
D) The purchase is void.

Key Takeaway
A slave's earnings belong to his master, so paying the master with them is ineffective; a second payment is wajib to fulfill the substitute for emancipation.

Slave's Agency to Purchase Himself: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof from Al-Hidayah's commentary on slave agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the agent owes a second payment when purchasing a slave without specification, and distinguish the ruling when the purchase is for the slave himself.

Core Idea
An agent buying a slave without specification becomes owner and must pay again; the slave's own purchase is emancipation with substitute.

When a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master, the transaction is legally complex because the slave's earnings belong to the master. This lesson unpacks the three-part ruling from Al-Hidayah: the agent's ownership, the fate of the first payment, and the obligation to pay a second price. We will also examine the objection and response regarding the case when the purchase is intended for the slave himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components of the ruling
The passage presents three legal consequences: (1) the agent becomes owner of the slave, (2) the one thousand rupees given by the slave to the agent belongs to the master, (3) the agent owes another one thousand rupees to the master. State each clearly.
These are not independent; they are linked logically.
2
Trace the proof for the third component
The author uses qiyas: the agent is obliged to pay the price (thaman). The money he gave was the slave's earnings, which already belong to the master, so it does not discharge the price obligation. Therefore, the agent must pay another thousand.
This reasoning relies on the principle that slave earnings are master's property.
3
Understand the objection and Qadi Khan's view
If the purchase was for the slave himself (i.e., the agent was supposed to buy for the slave), does the slave owe the second thousand? Qadi Khan says yes, because the first payment was already the master's, so the slave must provide a valid substitute.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; the majority ultimately holds the agent liable when he acts contrary to instructions.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response from al-Inayah
The author of al-Inayah argues that the text of al-Hidayah implies the slave owes the second thousand because the slave's purchase of himself is emancipation with a substitute. If the slave did not owe, it would be emancipation without substitute.
The key is that the slave's own purchase is legally impossible as a commutative contract, so it becomes emancipation.
5
Compare with normal agency and conclude
In normal agency, stating 'for my principal' is unnecessary; the contract is for the principal regardless. But here, the agent acted contrary to the slave's command (he made a pure purchase without specifying for the slave), so the contract binds the agent, making him owner and liable.
The distinction hinges on the agent's deviation from the principal's instructions.

اعتراض صاحب ہدایہ کے قول "وامكن العمل بها" پر ایک اعتراض ہے وہ یہ کہ آپ کا یہ فرمانا کہ اگر وکیل نے "اشتریت عبدک بالف" کہا اور یہ نہیں کہا کہ میں نے اس غلام کے واسطے اس کو خریدا ہے تو اس صورت میں حقیقت پر عمل کرنا ممکن ہے۔ ہمیں یہ بات تسلیم نہیں ہے کیونکہ غلام نے اپنے مولیٰ سے اپنی ذات کو خریدنے کا وکیل کیا ہے۔ اور اپنی ذات کو خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا عبد معین کو خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا ہے اور جب مؤکل عبد معین یا کسی شی معین کے خریدنے کا وکیل کرتا ہے تو وکیل اس معین کو اپنے واسطے خریدنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ پس یہاں بھی وکیل اس غلام معین کو اپنے واسطے خریدنے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا ۔ اور جب وکیل اس غلام کو اپنے واسطے خریدنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے تو اس غلام کی خریداری مؤکل یعنی خود اس غلام کیلئے ہوگی اور جب اس غلام کی خریداری خود اسی غلام کے واسطے ہے تو یہ عقد معاوضہ نہیں ہو سکتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ یہاں حقیقت یعنی معاوضہ کے معنی پر عمل کرنا ممکن نہیں ہے۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ موکل یعنی غلام نے وکیل کو جس تصرف کا وکیل کیا تھا وکیل نے اس کے مطابق تصرف نہیں کیا ہے بلکہ دوسرا تصرف کیا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ غلام نے وکیل سے یہ کہا تھا کہ تو مجھ کو میرے مولیٰ سے میری ذات کے واسطے ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خرید اور یہ اعتاق مال کی جنس ہے جیسا کہ پہلے گزر چکا ۔ مگر جب وکیل نے مولیٰ کے سامنے صرف یہ کہا کہ میں نے تیرا غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خرید لیا اور یہ نہیں کہا کہ اس غلام کے واسطے خریدا ہے تو یہ محض شراء اور خریداری ہوئی سے وکیل نے کیا ہے محض شراء کا ارتکاب کیا ہے تو یہ عمل، مؤکل ( غلام) کے حکم کے خلاف ہوا اور وکیل جب مؤکل کے حکم کے خلاف عمل کرتا ہے تو اس عمل کو وکیل پر نافذ کیا جاتا ہے نہ کہ مؤکل پر۔ پس جب غلام کی خریداری کا یہ عمل وکیل پر نافذ کیا گیا تو وکیل اس غلام کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ اور دوسرے جز ( وہ ایک ہزار روپیہ جو غلام نے اپنے وکیل کو دیا تھا مولی کیلئے ہوگا) کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ ایک ہزار روپیہ مولیٰ کے غلام
Book of Agency. Ashraf al-Bidaya volume - and his earnings are the earnings of the slave, and all earnings of the slave belong to the master. Therefore this one thousand rupees also will be for the master. And the proof of the third part, i.e., the proof that the buyer (agent) will owe another one thousand rupees, is that the buyer has a price (thaman) obligation, and the one thousand rupees that the buyer gave to the master is the earnings of his slave, and the slave's earnings belong to the master, so this one thousand rupees will not be considered payment of the slave's price, and it is not valid as payment of price. So when the buyer owes the price, and the one thousand rupees he gave to the master is not valid as payment, then he owes another one thousand rupees as the slave's price. The author of Fath al-Qadir, al-Inayah, and al-Kifayah said that the obligation of the second thousand upon the buyer is obvious when the purchase is for the buyer. But if the purchase is for the slave himself, such that the slave becomes free, then is the second thousand also obligatory upon the slave? In al-Jami' al-Saghir, Qadi Khan said that although this is not mentioned in the book, it is appropriate that the slave owes the second thousand to the master. Because the one thousand that the slave gave to his agent, and the agent gave to the master, was the slave's earnings and already the master's property, so it cannot be the substitute for emancipation. And when it cannot be the substitute, then the slave necessarily owes another one thousand as the substitute. The author of al-Inayah responded that the text of al-Hidayah indicates the obligation of the second thousand upon the slave, because the author of al-Hidayah said that the slave's purchase of himself is accepting emancipation in exchange for a substitute. So if the slave is not made to owe the second thousand, then this would be emancipation without a substitute, but it is established as emancipation with a substitute. So the author's making it emancipation with a substitute proves the obligation of the second thousand. In contrast, if someone other than a slave appoints an agent to buy a slave, e.g., Wasif appoints Shahid as agent to buy 'Arif's slave for him for one thousand, then it is not a condition for the agent to state at the time of purchase that he bought it for his principal. Because whether he says it or not, the contract of purchase will be for the principal, and both contracts—one attributed to the agent and one to the principal—are the same from the seller's perspective. In both cases the contract is a sale for the seller, and there is no emancipation from the seller's side, and the demand will be directed to the contracting party, i.e., the agent. So when both contracts are the same, and the demand and contractual rights fall upon the agent, stating 'I bought this slave for my principal' is absolutely unnecessary. In contrast, if the slave buys himself from his master, then because acting upon the literal meaning is impossible, it is construed metaphorically as emancipation. Acting literally is impossible because the slave is not capable of owning property. So his purchase of himself does not cause ownership, but is metaphorically emancipation, or because the slave is not property in relation to himself, and cannot own himself. The reason is that the slave is a human being in relation to himself, subject to hudud and qisas, and property is something else created for human benefit. Since the slave is not property in relation to himself, his purchase of himself cannot be a commutative contract, and when it cannot be commutative, it is not possible to act upon the meaning of commutative. When acting upon the literal meaning (commutative) is not possible, this purchase is metaphorically construed as emancipation for a consideration. The appropriateness is that both commutative contract (sale) and emancipation remove ownership. The only difference is that sale removes ownership but with a substitute, while emancipation removes ownership without a substitute necessarily. Another difference is that sale removes ownership from the seller and makes the buyer owner, while emancipation removes the master's ownership. In summary, if the slave bought himself, it is metaphorically emancipation. If the agent bought the slave from his master and did not say 'I bought this slave for him', then it is a commutative contract, meaning the agent (buyer) becomes owner of the slave. Objection to the statement of al-Hidayah 'and acting upon it is possible' is that you said if the agent said 'I bought your slave for one thousand' and did not say 'I bought it for this slave', then acting upon the literal meaning is possible. We do not accept this, because the slave appointed the agent to buy himself from his master, and appointing an agent to buy oneself is appointing to buy a specific slave. And when the principal appoints to buy a specific slave or specific thing, the agent is not authorized to buy it for himself. So here too the agent is not authorized to buy this specific slave for himself. Since the agent is not authorized to buy it for himself, the purchase is for the principal, i.e., the slave, and when the purchase is for the slave himself, it cannot be a commutative contract, as previously established. So it is proven that acting upon the literal meaning (commutative) is not possible. The answer is that the principal (the slave) authorized the agent for a certain transaction, but the agent did not act accordingly; rather, he performed a different transaction. Because the slave told the agent, 'Buy me from my master for myself for one thousand,' and this is a type of emancipation for consideration. But when the agent said only to the master, 'I bought your slave for one thousand,' and did not say 'for this slave,' this was merely a purchase, and the agent committed a mere purchase. This action was contrary to the principal's command, and when the agent acts contrary to the principal's command, the action is binding upon the agent, not the principal. So when this purchase of the slave is made binding upon the agent, the agent becomes the owner of the slave. And the proof of the second part (the one thousand that the slave gave to his agent will be for the master) is that this one thousand is from the master's slave...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof from the commentary on Al-Hidayah regarding the case of a slave appointing an agent to purchase him from his master. The author breaks down the scenario into three parts: (1) the agent becomes owner of the slave if he does not specify buying for the slave; (2) the one thousand rupees given by the slave to the agent becomes the master's property; (3) the agent owes another one thousand rupees to the master. The third part is proven by the fact that the agent has an obligation to pay the price, but the money he gave was the slave's earnings belonging to the master, so it does not count as payment, requiring a second payment. The passage then discusses an objection: what if the purchase is for the slave himself? Qadi Khan opines that the slave still owes the second thousand because the first thousand was already the master's. The author of al-Inayah argues that the text of al-Hidayah implies the slave owes the second thousand because the slave's purchase of himself is emancipation with a substitute. Then a comparison is made with a normal agency where the agent is not required to state 'for my principal' because the contract is valid for the principal regardless, and the agent is liable for the rights. In the slave's case, however, if the slave buys himself directly, it is impossible to act literally because a slave cannot own property, so it is construed as emancipation. Therefore, when an agent acts contrary to the slave's instruction (i.e., by buying without specifying for the slave), the contract is for the agent himself, making him owner. The objection and response clarify that the agent's action contradicts the principal's order, so it binds the agent, not the slave. The commentary follows the author's order, first presenting the three-part ruling, then the objection, then the response, all while maintaining the Hanafi legal framework.

Key Terms
وکالت (wakalah): A contract where one person appoints another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
وکیل (wakil): The agent who acts on behalf of the principal in a transaction.
اعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave, which removes the master's ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the agent owe a second thousand rupees to the master?
A) Because the slave's earnings belong to the agent, so the first payment is invalid.
B) Because the first payment was the slave's earnings, which already belong to the master, so it cannot count as the price.
C) Because the agent acted without permission, so he must pay a penalty.
D) Because the master did not receive any payment.

Key Takeaway
An agent who buys a slave without specification becomes owner and must pay the full price again, because the first payment (slave's earnings) already belongs to the master.

Agent Buying Slave Without Specification: Literal Exchange vs. Emancipation
Ruling when agent buys slave from master without specifying emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys a slave from the master without specifying emancipation, and explain the linguistic principle that governs the interpretation of 'ishtara'.

Core Idea
Without explicit intent, purchase defaults to exchange – literal meaning of 'ishtara' governs.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies a critical nuance in agency and slave transactions. When a wakil (agent) buys a slave from his master without stating that he is buying for the slave's emancipation, the Hanafi school rules that the slave becomes the agent's property. Why? Because the word 'ishtara' (to buy) is literally a contract of exchange (mu'awada), and unless specified otherwise, it must be taken literally. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including the contrast with a slave buying himself (where literal interpretation is impossible) and with an agent buying from a third party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is an agent buying a slave from the master without specifying the purpose. The ruling: the slave becomes the property of the buyer (agent).
The agent is the buyer in the contract.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is linguistic: 'لأن اللفظ حقيقة للمعاوضة' – the word 'ishtara' is a literal for exchange. This is a qiyas based on linguistic usage.
The dalil is not a direct text from Quran or Sunnah but a rational argument from language.
3
Apply the principle of literal interpretation (haqiqah)
Since the agent did not specify a metaphorical intent (emancipation), the literal meaning is preserved. The Hanafi usul principle: literal meaning must be applied when possible (al-haqiqah muqaddamah ala al-majaz).
This contrasts with the case of a slave buying himself, where literal is impossible.
4
Contrast with the exception: slave buying himself
In the slave's self-purchase, literal interpretation would lead to absurdity (self-ownership), so the metaphorical meaning (emancipation) is forced. Here, no such absurdity exists.
The exception proves the rule: when literal is possible, it must be used.
5
Note the practical consequence
The slave belongs to the agent, not the master. The price is a debt on the agent, and the master receives the payment. The master cannot later demand the slave back.
This ruling protects the contract's integrity and avoids ambiguity.

راون ہے اسلئے کرنا جائز نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں چونکہ وکیل نے یہ نہیں کہا کہ میں نے تیرا یہ نام خود اس غلام کے واسطے خیر اسلئے وکیل سے کام اشتريت عبدک بالف کی حقیقت پر عمل کرنا ممکن ہے اور جب اس کام کی حقیقت پر عمل کرناممکن ہے تو اس کی حقیقت میں معاوضہ نے کی پوری پوری حفاظت کی جائے گی۔ اور جب وکیل کی مذکورہ خریداری ، معاوضہ ہے اور معاوضہ پر عمل بھی کیا گیا تو اس وکیل کیلئے نام پر ملک ثابت ہو جائے گی اور وکیل ہی اس نام کا مالک ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ عقد معاوضہ وکیل اور نام کے بائع کے درمیان ہی منہ ران منتقد منتقد معاوضه به چونکه مشتری ہے اور مشترکی میچ کا مالک ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے وکیل ہیچ یعنی نام کا مالک ہوگا۔
And if he does not explain to the master, then he (the slave) is a slave of the buyer because the word (ishtara) is a literal for exchange, and it is possible to act upon it when he does not explain, so it is preserved, unlike the slave buying himself because the metaphorical meaning is determined there. And if it is a exchange, ownership is established for him, and the thousand [dirhams] are for the master because he has the earnings of his slave, and the buyer owes a thousand like it as a price for the slave, for it is in his liability since the payment did not take place, unlike the agent buying the slave from someone else where his statement is not required because the two contracts there are of one pattern, and in both cases the claim is directed toward the contractor. As for here, one of them is an emancipation that entails wala', and there is no claim on the agent, and the master may not be pleased and may desire pure exchange, so the statement is necessary.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling from the Hanafi school when an agent (wakil) buys a slave from his master without specifying that the purchase is for the slave's own emancipation. The key principle is that the verb 'ishtara' (to buy) is a literal term for a contract of exchange (mu'awada). Since the agent did not clarify that he is buying the slave for the slave's benefit (to free him), the literal meaning must be applied: it is a sale for consideration. Consequently, the slave becomes the property of the buyer (the agent) because in a valid exchange, ownership transfers to the purchaser. The price is on the agent's liability, not the master's, because the payment was not made properly. The passage contrasts this with the case where the slave buys himself directly—there, the word 'ishtara' cannot be taken literally because it would imply the slave owning himself, which is impossible, so it must be interpreted as emancipation (majaz). Also, it compares to an agent buying from a third party, where no such statement is needed because both contracts are of the same type. Here, because one contract (if interpreted as emancipation) would create wala' (clientage) and the agent has no claim, while the master may not consent to emancipation, it is essential for the agent to state his intention clearly.

Key Terms
معاوضة (mu'awada): A contract of exchange where each party gives and receives something of value.
اعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation; freeing a slave, which is a metaphorical interpretation of 'ishtara' when a slave buys himself.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning; the original, non-metaphorical usage of a word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the status of a slave bought by an agent from the master without any specification of emancipation?
A) The slave becomes free immediately.
B) The slave becomes the property of the agent.
C) The slave remains the property of the master.
D) The slave becomes the property of the principal (the person who appointed the agent).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when an agent buys a slave from the master without specifying emancipation, the literal meaning of purchase as exchange applies, making the agent the owner of the slave.

Slave Buying Himself Through Agent with Specification
Ruling when slave instructs agent to buy him for himself and agent specifies this to master

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave instructs an agent to buy him from his master and the agent specifies the purchase is for the slave, and explain the reasoning using qiyas.

Core Idea
When a slave buys himself via an explicit agent, it is emancipation, not sale—no one can own themselves.

This lesson expands on the foundational rule that a slave cannot own himself. Here, the slave uses an agent, but the agent's clear statement makes the transaction an emancipation. We will follow the qiyas from direct self-purchase to agent-assisted purchase and understand the resulting legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
First, understand the scenario: a slave asks an agent to buy him from the master for a price. The agent explicitly tells the master 'I bought him for himself.' The master sells on that condition. The Hanafi ruling is that the slave becomes free and wala' belongs to the master.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the original case (asl) and its ruling
The asl is a slave buying himself directly from the master. In that case, the ruling is also emancipation because a person cannot own themselves. The proof is the impossibility of self-ownership.
This is established by necessity rather than explicit textual evidence.
3
Apply qiyas: find the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that the agent is a mere messenger (safir) whose actions are attributed to the principal. In this scenario, because the agent specified the purchase is for the slave, the contract is attributed to the slave, just as if he had acted personally. This illa is jaliyyah (clear) and extracted by tahqiq al-manat.
The agent's explicit statement is crucial; without it, the agent would be a principal, not a safir.
4
Derive the ruling for the new case (far')
Since the illa applies (agent is safir, contract attributed to the slave), the ruling from the asl (emancipation) transfers to the far'. Therefore, the transaction is emancipation, the slave is free, and wala' goes to the master.
The hukm includes both freedom and the right of patronage.
5
Note the practical consequence and any differences
The practical consequence is that the slave is free and the master retains wala' (right of inheritance). If the agent had not specified, the transaction would be a regular sale, and the slave would remain enslaved. This difference is the thamarah al-khilaf.
Other schools might treat it as a sale, but Hanafi views it as emancipation due to the impossibility of self-ownership.

Source Text

قال واذا قال العبد لرجل اشتر لى نفسى من مولاى بالف ودفعها اليه فان قال الرجل للمولى اشتريته لنفسه قباعه على هذا فهو حر والولاء للمولى لان بيع نفس العبد منه اعتاق وشراء العبد نفسه قبول الاعتاق ببدل والمامور سفير عنه اذ لا يرجع عليه الحقوق فصار كانه اشترى بنفسه واذا كان اعتاقا اعقب الولاء
ترجمہ امام محمد نے کہا کہ اگر غلام نے کسی آدمی سے کہا کہ تو مجھے کو میرے لئے مولیٰ سے ایک ہزار کے عوض خرید اور غلام نے اس آدمی کو ایک ہزار دے دیئے۔ پس اگر اس آدمی (وکیل) نے اس کے مولیٰ سے کہا کہ میں نے اس غلام کو اس کی ذات کے واسطے خریدا ہے۔ پس مولی نے اس غلام کو اس شرط پر فروخت کیا تو وہ غلام آزاد ہے اور اس کی ولاء اس کے مالک کیلئے ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ غلام کی ذات کو غلام کے ہاتھ فروخت کرنا اعتناق ہے اور غلام کا اپنی ذات کو خریدنا اس اعتناق کو بالعوض قبول کرنا ہے اور وکیل غلام کی طرف سے محض سفیر ہے کیونکہ وکیل پر حقوق راج نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسے غلام نے اپنی ذات کو بذات خود خریدا ہے اور جب یہ بیع اعتناق ہوئی تو اس کے بعد ولاء ثابت ہوگی ۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک غلام نے کسی آدمی سے کہا کہ تو مجھ کو میرے لئے میرے مولیٰ سے ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خرید اور غلام نے اس وکیل کو ایک ہزار روپیہ دے دیئے۔ پس اگر وکیل نے اس غلام کے مولیٰ سے یہ کہا کہ میں نے اس غلام کو اسی کے واسطے خریدا ہے اور مولیٰ نے اس غلام کو اسی طریقہ پر فروخت بھی کر دیا تو یہ غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس غلام کی ولاء مولی کیلئے ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مولی کا غلام کی ذات کو غلام کے ہاتھ فروخت کرنا مولیٰ کی طرف سے اعتاق علی مال ہے۔ یعنی جب مولی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے ہاتھ تیری ذات کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض فروخت کیا تو اس کا مطالبہ ہے کہ میں نے تجھ کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض آزاد کر دیا ہے۔ بہر حال مولی کی طرف سے یہ بیج اعتاق علی مال ہے۔ اور اعتاق علی مال ، غلام کے قبول کرنے پر موقوف ہوتا ہے مگر جب غلام نے اپنی ذات کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خرید لیا تو یہ خریداری غلام کی طرف سے ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض اعتاق کو قبول کرنا ہوگا۔ اور رہا وکیل تو وہ غلام (مؤکل) کی طرف سے محض سفیر ہے اسی وجہ سے اس عقد میں وکیل کی طرف حقوق عقد
راج نہیں ہوتے۔
اور وجہ یہ ہے کہ وکیل نے عقد کو اپنے مؤکل یعنی غلام کی طرف منسوب کیا ہے، پس جب وکیل نے عقد کو اپنے مؤکل یعنی غلام کی طرف منسوب کر دیا ہے تو اس نے اپنے آپ کو محض سفیر اور ایچی قرار دیا۔ اور جب ایسا ہے تو گویا غلام نے اپنی ذات کو بذات خود بغیر کسی واسطہ کے خریدا ہے۔ بہر حال جب مولی کا غلام کی ذات کو غلام کے ہاتھ فروخت کرنا اعتاق ہے تو اس کے بعد ولا معتق ( آزاد کرنے والا ) یعنی مولیٰ کیلئے ثابت ہوگی۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ اس صورت میں غلام آزاد ہے اور اس کی ولاء اس کے مولیٰ کیلئے ثابت ہوگی ۔
He (Imam Muhammad) said: If a slave says to a man, 'Buy me for myself from my master for a thousand,' and gives it to him, and the man says to the master, 'I bought him for himself,' and he sells him on this condition, then he is free and the wala' belongs to the master. Because selling the slave himself from him is emancipation, and the slave's buying himself is acceptance of emancipation for a compensation, and the commissioned person is a mere messenger on his behalf since rights do not revert upon him. So it is as if he bought himself. And since it is emancipation, it entails wala'.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage details another ruling from Imam Muhammad regarding a slave who asks an agent to buy him from his master for a price, and the agent explicitly states to the master that he is buying the slave for the slave's own sake. When the master sells on this condition, the transaction is legally considered an emancipation (i'taq 'ala mal) rather than a sale. The reasoning is that the master selling the slave to the slave himself is deemed emancipation because a person cannot own himself. The slave's action of 'buying' is actually acceptance of emancipation in exchange for payment. The agent is merely a messenger (safir) because the rights of the contract (like ownership) do not revert to him; they go to the slave. Therefore, it is as if the slave bought himself directly. Consequently, the slave becomes free, and the wala' (right of inheritance and patronage) belongs to the master (the emancipator). The passage emphasizes that the agent's clear statement to the master is crucial for this ruling.

Key Terms
سفير (safir): A mere messenger or intermediary whose actions are attributed to the principal, not to himself.
ولاء (wala'): The right of inheritance and patronage that belongs to the emancipator of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave instructs an agent to buy him for himself from his master for 1000 dirhams, and the agent says to the master, 'I bought him for himself,' what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave remains a slave because the agent did not own the price.
B) The slave becomes free and the wala' goes to the master.
C) The slave becomes the property of the agent.
D) The transaction is invalid because a slave cannot buy himself.

Key Takeaway
When a slave attempts to buy himself through an agent, the outcome hinges on the agent's explicit declaration; if he says 'for himself,' it is emancipation with wala' for the master.

The Oath of the Agent and the Principal in a Price Dispute
Resolving conflicting views on whether the agent's oath suffices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the oath of the agent and principal in a price dispute and evaluate the argument for requiring mutual oaths (tahāluf) by analyzing the seller's status as a stranger.

Core Idea
The agent's oath does not suffice; mutual oaths (tahāluf) are required because the seller is a stranger to the principal.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi debate on whether the agent's oath alone resolves a price dispute with the principal. Imam Muhammad's statement that 'the statement is the statement of the commanded person with his oath' seems to suggest the agent's word is accepted. However, Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the author of al-Hidayah argue that mutual oaths (tahāluf) are necessary. We will trace the reasoning and see how the seller's status as a stranger forces the application of tahāluf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State Imam Muhammad's view that the agent's oath suffices because his statement is prima facie accepted, and the opposing view of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi that mutual oaths are required.
Note that both are within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand Imam Muhammad's proof
Imam Muhammad relies on the general principle 'the statement is the statement of the commanded person with his oath' and the priority that the agent's oath implies the principal's oath by priority.
The principal as defendant is even more deserving of his oath.
3
See the objection
A critic objects that tahāluf rescinds the contract without accepting anyone's statement, whereas Imam Muhammad's principle seems to accept the agent's statement.
This creates an apparent contradiction.
4
Analyze the Hidayah response
The author of al-Hidayah responds that the seller, after receiving the price, becomes a stranger to the principal, so his confirmation is not admissible. Thus the disagreement remains, requiring tahāluf.
The seller is a stranger both before and after receipt of the price.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of al-Hidayah supports tahāluf, which is the relied-upon position (muftā bihi) for fatwa.
Thus in practice, both the agent and principal must swear oaths and the contract is rescinded.

كتاب الوكالة
الشرف الہدایہ جلد - وہم حاصل یہ کہ امام محمد نے معظم یمین یعنی بائع وکیل کی قسم کے ذکر پر اکتفا کیا ہے اور اس بات کی طرف اشارہ کیا ہے کہ جب وکیل ( جو مدعی ہے ) پر قسم واجب ہے تو موکل ( جو منکر ہے ) پر بدرجہ اولی قسم واجب ہوگی یعنی امام محمد نے اگر چہ صرف وکیل کی قسم کو ذکر کیا ہے۔ لیکن مراد موکل کی قسم بھی ہے اور جب دونوں کی قسم مراد ہے تو امام محمد کی ذکر کردہ تصریح ، اور شیخ ابو منصور ماتریدی کے قول میں کوئی تعارض واقع نہ ہوگا۔
لیکن اس جواب پر بھی اشکال ہے۔ وہ یہ کہ امام محمد کا قول فالقول قول المامور مع یمینہ اس بات پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ وکیل نے جو کچھ کہا کہ میں نے ایک ہزار میں خریدا ہے۔ اس میں وکیل کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ حالانکہ تحالف میں کسی کے قول کی تصدیق نہیں کی جاتی ہے بلکہ عقد کو فتح کر دیا جاتا ہے۔ پس امام محمد کی مراد تحالف ہوتا تو امام محمد فالقول قول المامور مع يمينه دفرماتے۔ حاصل یہ کہ شیخ ابو منصور ماتریدی کا مخالف کا قول امام محمد کی تصریح کے خلاف ہے۔
والبائع بعد استيفاء الثمن اجنبي عنها ... الخ سے صاحب ہدایہ نے شیخ ابو منصور ماتریدی کی طرف سے قول اول کی دلیل لانہ ارتفع الخلاف بتصدیق البائع کا جواب دیا ہے۔
جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا بائع من وصول کرنے کے بعد مؤکل اور وکیل دونوں سے اجنبی ہو گیا اور ثمن وصول کرنے سے پہلے مؤکل سے اجنبی تھا کیونکہ بائع اور مؤکل کے درمیان کوئی عقد منعقد نہیں ہوا ہے۔ الغرض بائع دونوں صورتوں میں موکل سے اجنبی ہے اور جب بائع موکل سے اجنبی ہے تو بائع کا کلام موکل کے خلاف معتبر نہ ہوگا اور اس کی تصدیق نہ کی جائے گی۔ اور جب بائع کا کلام معتبر نہ رہا اور اس کی تصدیق نہ کی گئی تو مقدار ثمن میں موکل اور وکیل کے درمیان اختلاف باقی رہا اور جب مقدار ثمن میں اختلاف باقی رہا تو ان دونوں کے درمیان تحالف واجب ہوگا ۔ اور ان دونوں کے درمیان واقع شدہ عقد کی فتح کر دیا جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب جمیل احمد غفر له
Book of Agency. The gist is that Imam Muhammad sufficed with mentioning the oath of the most prominent party, i.e., the seller agent, and indicated that when the agent (who is the claimant) is required to take an oath, then the principal (who is the defendant) is even more deserving of the oath. Imam Muhammad only mentioned the agent's oath, but the principal's oath is also intended. When both oaths are intended, there is no contradiction between Imam Muhammad's explicit statement and the saying of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. But there is an objection to this answer. It is that Imam Muhammad's saying 'The statement is the statement of the commanded person with his oath' indicates that the agent's claim of purchasing for a thousand is accepted, whereas in mutual oaths, no one's statement is accepted; rather, the contract is rescinded. If Imam Muhammad meant mutual oaths, he would have said 'The statement is the statement of the commanded person with his oath'. The conclusion is that the opposing view of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi contradicts Imam Muhammad's explicit statement. And the statement 'And the seller, after receiving the price, is a stranger to her...' etc. The author of al-Hidayah responded to the first opinion of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi with the proof that the disagreement is removed by the seller's confirmation. The gist of the answer is that the slave's seller, after receiving the price, becomes a stranger to both the principal and the agent, and before receiving the price, he was a stranger to the principal because no contract was concluded between the seller and the principal. In short, the seller is a stranger to the principal in both cases. When the seller is a stranger to the principal, the seller's statement is not admissible against the principal, nor is he confirmed. When the seller's statement is not admissible and not confirmed, the disagreement between the principal and the agent regarding the amount of the price remains. When the disagreement over the price remains, mutual oaths between them become obligatory, and the contract concluded between them is rescinded. And Allah knows best what is correct. Jamil Ahmad, may he be forgiven.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the respective oaths of the agent and principal when the price of a purchased item is disputed. The commentator, derived from Ashraf al-Hidayah, is resolving an apparent conflict between Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's statement 'the statement is the statement of the commanded person with his oath' and the view of Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, who argued that mutual oaths (tahāluf) are required. The key point is that Imam Muhammad, by only mentioning the agent's oath, implicitly includes the principal's oath by priority: since the agent is the claimant, his oath is required first, and the principal, as defendant, is even more worthy of being believed with his oath. A critic objects that tahāluf nullifies the contract, whereas the agent's oath here would confirm his statement. The author from al-Hidayah responds by noting that the seller, being a stranger to the principal, cannot testify against him, so the disagreement persists, necessitating tahāluf. This is a nuanced discussion of evidence and oaths in sales agency.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties to a contract when they disagree over the price or other terms, leading to rescission of the contract.
اجنبي (ajnabī): A stranger, i.e., someone who has no contractual relationship with the principal, so their statement is not admissible against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the seller's statement not resolve the dispute between the principal and the agent?
A) Because the seller is a slave.
B) Because the seller is a stranger to the principal, so his word is not binding.
C) Because the seller is the one who bought the item.
D) Because the seller already received the price.

Key Takeaway
In a price dispute between principal and agent, if the seller is a stranger to the principal, mutual oaths (tahāluf) are wajib and the contract is rescinded.

Rulings When a Slave is Appointed to Purchase Himself
Explaining three scenarios of a slave acting in self-purchase agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of a slave acting as agent to purchase himself and explain the ruling that the slave becomes free in each.

Core Idea
An agent's opposition to the principal's command makes the transaction for the agent, freeing the slave if he owns nothing.

This lesson from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines cases where a slave is appointed to purchase himself, either as principal or as agent. We focus on three scenarios where the slave's actions deviate from the principal's instructions, resulting in the slave's freedom through 'itq 'alā māl (emancipation for payment).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of agency
Distinguish between the slave as principal (appointing another to buy him) and the slave as agent (appointed by another to buy himself). The text focuses on the latter.
The first form: slave appoints a man to buy him from his master. The second: a man appoints the slave to buy himself for that man.
2
Analyze the first scenario: slave-agent opposes command
The slave was told to buy himself for the principal, but instead asked his master for emancipation for payment. Since he opposed, the purchase is binding on him, and as he owns nothing, it becomes 'itq 'alā māl, freeing him.
Key principle: agent's opposition to the principal's command makes the transaction for the agent.
3
Understand the second scenario
The text alludes to a scenario where the slave says 'Sell my self to me' and it is interpreted as for himself, leading to freedom.
Not fully detailed, but similar to the third scenario in outcome.
4
Examine the third scenario: absolute language
The slave says 'Sell my self to me' without specifying for whom. The absolute statement is ambiguous; it cannot be compliance with the principal's command, so it is for the slave himself, resulting in freedom.
The ambiguity creates doubt, and the slave is treated as acting for himself.
5
Conclude the ruling
In all scenarios where the slave does not clearly comply with the principal's command, the transaction is for the slave, and he becomes free via 'itq 'alā māl.
This is the muftā bihī position in the Hanafi school.

جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ اس مسئلہ میں وکیل نے موکل کے حکم کی مخالفت کی ہے اس طور پر کہ غلام کو اس بات کا وکیل کیا گیا تھا کہ وہ اپنی ذات کو مال کے عوض خرید کر موکل کا مملوک بنادے مگر غلام نے ایسا نہیں کیا۔ بلکہ اس نے اعتاق علی مال کا تصرف کیا ہے۔ یعنی اپنے مولیٰ سے یہ کہا کہ آپ مجھے مال کے عوض آزاد کر دیں۔ اور یہ بات پہلے گزر چکی ہے کہ اگر وکیل نے موکل کے حکم کی مخالفت کی ہو تو خریداری خود وکیل پر نافذ ہوتی ہے۔

اور اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا یہ کلام یعنی نفسی مطلق ہے۔ دونوں صورتوں کا احتمال ہے یعنی یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ غلام نے اپنی ذات کو اپنے واسطے خریدا ہوا اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ اپنے موکل کے واسطے خریدا ہو۔ پس جب دونوں احتمال بی تو غلام کا یہ کلام مشکوک ہوا۔ اور کلام مشکوک چونکہ حکم کی تعمیل قرار نہیں دیا جا سکتا ہے۔ اسلئے شک کی وجہ سے غلام کا یہ کلام موکل کے حکم کی تعمیل واقع نہ ہوگا۔ یعنی یہ نہیں کہا جائے گا کہ غلام نے موکل کے حکم کی تعمیل کی ہے اور اپنی ذات کو موکل کے واسطے خریدا ہے۔ اور جب غلام کا یہ کلام مؤکل کے حکم کی تعمیل واقع نہیں ہوا تو غلام کا یہ تصرف یعنی اپنی ذات کو خریدنا خود غلام کیو اسطے واقع ہوگا ۔ اور اس تصرف سے غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ جیسا کہ سابق میں گذر چکا ہے کیونکہ ظاہر یہی ہے کہ انسان اپنے واسطے تصرف کرے۔ خاص طور پر ایسا تصرف جس سے اعتاق حاصل ہوتا ہے۔ بہر حال ثابت ہو گیا کہ غلام اس تیسری صورت میں بھی آزاد ہو جائے گا۔
Chapter on appointing an agent to purchase the slave himself. Translation: Chapter on appointing an agent to purchase the slave himself. Explanation: Appointing an agent to purchase the slave himself has two forms: the alif-lam on 'al-tawkīl' is in place of the possessive, and 'tawkīl' is a verbal noun in genitive. First form: So one form is that the verbal noun is attributed to the agent, the implied phrase is 'the slave appoints a man as agent from his master,' i.e., the slave appoints a man to purchase him from his master. This is the first issue in this chapter. Second form: The verbal noun is attributed to the object, the implied phrase is 'a man appoints the slave as agent,' i.e., a man appoints the slave to purchase himself from his master for that man. This is the second issue in this chapter. In the first issue, the slave is the principal; in the second, the slave is the agent. The author's statement 'fī al-tawkīl bi sharā' nafs'. [page 248] Answer: The answer is that in this issue, the agent (the slave) opposed the command of the principal. He was appointed to purchase himself for the principal, but instead he performed an act of 'itq 'alā māl, i.e., he said to his master: "Free me for payment." It has been established earlier that if the agent opposes the principal's command, the purchase is binding on the agent. Therefore, in this issue, the purchase is binding on the agent, i.e., the slave. Since the slave owns nothing, this purchase becomes an 'itq 'alā māl, and the slave becomes free. And likewise, if the slave said: "Sell my self to me" and did not say "for so-and-so", he is free. Because the absolute statement can have two meanings, so it is not considered compliance due to doubt; thus the transaction remains for himself. Translation: And similarly, if the slave said: "Sell my self to me" and did not say "for so-and-so", he is free. Because the absolute statement can have two meanings, so it is not considered compliance due to doubt; thus the transaction remains for himself. Explanation: In this phrase, the third of the three mentioned scenarios is mentioned. The gist is that the slave left the contract absolute, i.e., he did not attribute it to himself nor to his principal. Rather, the slave said to his master: "Sell my self to me" without any addition. In this case too, the slave becomes free. The proof for this is that the slave's statement 'nafsī' is absolute; it can mean either that the slave bought himself for himself or for his principal. When both possibilities exist, the slave's statement is doubtful. A doubtful statement cannot be considered compliance with the command. Therefore, due to doubt, the slave's statement is not compliance with the principal's command; i.e., it will not be said that the slave complied and bought himself for the principal. Since this statement is not compliance, the slave's transaction of buying himself is considered for himself. Thus, the slave becomes free, as established earlier, because it is evident that a person acts for himself, especially in a transaction that results in emancipation. In any case, it is proven that the slave becomes free in this third scenario too.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the chapter on appointing an agent to purchase a specific slave ('ayn al-ʿabd). The author first distinguishes two forms: (1) the slave appoints a man to buy him from his master (slave as principal), and (2) a man appoints the slave to buy himself for that man (slave as agent). After establishing these, the commentator discusses three scenarios. The first scenario is not explicitly detailed here; the text jumps to a case where the slave (as agent) contradicts his principal's instructions: instead of buying himself for the principal, he asks his master to free him for a payment. Since the agent violated the command, the purchase binds the agent himself. As the slave owns nothing, this becomes an 'itq 'alā māl (emancipation for payment), freeing the slave. The second scenario, also not fully detailed, is alluded to. The third scenario is the slave using absolute language: 'Sell my self to me' without specifying for whom. Because the expression is ambiguous and could be for himself or the principal, doubt prevents compliance with the principal's command. The transaction is therefore considered for the slave's own benefit, resulting in his freedom. The key principle: an agent's ambiguous statement that does not clearly comply with the principal's command is treated as the agent's own act.

Key Terms
اعتق علی مال (itq 'alā māl): Emancipation of a slave in exchange for payment, typically when the slave agrees to pay a sum to the master for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third scenario, why does the slave become free?
A) Because the master agreed to free him.
B) Because the slave's absolute statement could not be compliance with the principal's command, so it was for himself.
C) Because the principal cancelled the agency.
D) Because the slave had money to pay.

Key Takeaway
A slave appointed to purchase himself becomes free if he opposes the principal's command or uses ambiguous language, as the transaction is considered for himself and results in 'itq 'alā māl.

With Whom the Agent May Contract: The Tuhmah Dispute
Dispute on whether wakeel can contract with close relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the agent's dealings with close relatives and explain how the concept of tuhmah shapes the ruling.

Core Idea
Tuhmah (suspicion) blocks the agent from dealing with those whose testimony would be rejected.

This passage examines the boundaries of an agent's authority in sales and purchases. While an agency is generally absolute, the Hanafi school recognizes that certain relationships raise a suspicion of bias (tuhmah) that can undermine the fairness of the transaction. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples turns on whether tuhmah is inherent in all close-relative dealings or only where the agent has a direct pecuniary interest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the restricted persons
List the persons mentioned: father, grandfather, and those whose testimony is not accepted for the agent (e.g., son, wife, slave, mukatib).
These are the 'places of suspicion' (mawadi' tuhmah).
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa prohibits contracting with any of these persons. The Sahibayn permit it (at fair value) except for the agent's own slave and mukatib.
The Sahibayn's exception focuses on direct ownership interest.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa analogizes to testimony: just as testimony from such persons is rejected, so should agency be restricted. The Sahibayn argue the agency is absolute and properties are separate.
The qiyas (analogy) is based on the illa of tuhmah.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the agent cannot sell to these relatives at all. Under the Sahibayn, he can sell at market value, but not to his own slave or mukatib.
The thamarah (fruit) is whether the transaction is valid or void.
5
Recognize the extension
The same difference applies to contracts of ijarah (hire) and sarf (currency exchange).
This shows the principle is general across transaction types.

فصل فى البيع ترجمہ ( یہ ) فصل تو کیل بالبیع کے بیان میں ہے۔ تشریح توکیل بالشراء کی تمام انواع کے بیان سے فراغت کے بعد مصنف اس فصل میں تو کیل بالمیچ کے احکام ذکر فرما رہے نہیں ۔ اور تو کیل بالشراء کو تو کیل بالبیع پر مقدم کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ توکیل بالشراء اثبات کے معنی کو متضمن ہے بایں طور کہ شراء کے ذریعہ مبیع کو حاصل کیا جاتا ہے اور توکیل با لیچ ازالہ کے معنی کو ضمن ہے بایں طور کہ تو کیل بالصحیح کے ذریعہ مبیع کو اپنے سے زائل کرنا مقصود ہوتا ہے۔ اور ازالہ چونکہ وجودا اثبات کے بعد ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے وصفا بھی ازالہ یعنی توکیل با سبیع کو اثبات یعنی توکیل بالشراء سے بعد میں ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ وکیل بالبيع والشراء کن لوگوں کیساتھ عقد کر سکتا ہے کن کیساتھ نہیں قال والوكيل بالبيع والشراء لا يجوز له أن يعقد مع ابيه وجده ومن لا يقبل شهادته له عند ابي حنيفة و قالا يجوز بيعه منهم بمثل القيمة الامن عبده او مكاتبه لان التوكيل مطلق ولا تهمة اذا الاملاك متباينة والمنافع منقطعة بخلاف العبد لانه بيع من نفسه لان ما فى يد العبد للمولى وكذا للمولى حق في كسب المكاتب وينقلب حقيقة بالعجز وله أن مواضع التهمة مستثناة عن الوكالات ولهذا موضع التهمة بدليل عدم قبول الشهادة ولان المنافع بينهم متصلة فصار بيعاً من نفسه من وجه والاجارة والصرف على هذا الخلاف ترجمہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک وکیل بالمیچ اور وکیل بالشراء کیلئے جائز نہیں ہے کہ وہ اپنے باپ دادا کے ساتھ عقد کرے یا اس شخص کے ساتھ کرے جس کی گواہی اس کے حق میں قبول نہ کی جائے۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ ان لوگوں کے ہاتھ وکیل کا پوری قیمت پر بیچنا جائز ہے۔ مگر اپنے غلام یا مکاتب کے ہاتھ بیچنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے کہ تو کیل مطلق ہے اور کوئی تہمت نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ املاک جدا ہیں اور منافع منقطع ہیں۔ برخلاف غلام کے کیونکہ یہ اپنے ہاتھ فروخت کرنا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جو کچھ غلام کے قبضہ میں ہے وہ مولیٰ کی ملک ہے۔ اسی طرح مکاتب کی کمائی میں مولی کا حق ہوتا ہے۔ اور وہ حق مکاتب کے بدل کتابت ادا کرنے سے عاجز ہونے کی وجہ سے حقیقت ملک ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مواضع تہمت وکالتوں سے مستثنیٰ ہوتے ہیں اور یہ بھی موضع تہمت ہے۔ اس دلیل سے کہ ان لوگوں کی گواہی قبول نہیں ہوتی ہے اور اسلئے کہ ان میں منافع باہم متصل ہیں پس یہ مین وجہ اپنے ہاتھ بیع ہوگئی اور عقد اجارہ اور عقد صرف بھی اسی اختلاف پر ہیں۔ تشریح صاحب قدوری نے فرمایا کہ اگر ایک شخص نے کسی کو بیچنے یا خرید نے یا دونوں کا وکیل کیا تو حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک وکیل نہ اپنے باپ کے ساتھ عقد کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا۔ نہ دادا کے ساتھ اور نہ قرابت داروں کے ساتھ جن کے حق میں قبول نہ کی جاتی ہو۔ جیسے اس کا بیٹا ، پوتا، اور اس کی بیوی اور اس کا غلام اور مکاتب یہی امام شافعی کا ایک قول ہے اور ایک روایت میں امام مالک اور امام احمد بھی
Section on Sale. Translation: This is a section concerning agency for sale. Commentary: After finishing the description of all types of agency for purchase, the author now mentions the rulings of agency for sale. The reason for giving precedence to agency for purchase over agency for sale is that agency for purchase involves meaning of acquisition—through purchase the object is obtained—while agency for sale involves meaning of disposal—through agency for sale, one intends to remove the object from oneself. Since disposal exists after acquisition, in description too disposal (agency for sale) is mentioned after acquisition (agency for purchase). With whom the agent for sale and purchase may contract and with whom not. He said: The agent for sale and purchase is not permitted to contract with his father, grandfather, or one whose testimony is not accepted in his favor according to Abu Hanifa. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible for him to sell to them at a fair price, except for his own slave or mukatib, because the agency is absolute and there is no suspicion since the properties are separate and the benefits are disconnected—unlike the slave, because it is a sale from himself, as whatever is in the slave's hand belongs to the master, and likewise the master has a right in the earnings of the mukatib, which becomes actual ownership upon the mukatib's inability to pay. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that places of suspicion are exempt from agencies, and this is a place of suspicion, evidenced by the non-acceptance of testimony, and because benefits among them are connected, so it becomes a sale from himself in a sense. Ijarah and sarf are subject to this same difference. Commentary: Sahib al-Quduri said: If a person appoints someone as an agent for sale or purchase or both, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the agent is not permitted to contract with his father, nor with his grandfather, nor with relatives for whom his testimony is not accepted, such as his son, grandson, his wife, his slave, and his mukatib. This is also one saying of Imam al-Shafi'i, and in one narration, Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad (also hold it).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on agency for sale and then presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding with whom the agent for sale or purchase may contract. Abu Hanifa prohibits the agent from contracting with his father, grandfather, or anyone whose testimony is not accepted in his favor (e.g., son, wife, slave, mukatib) because such relationships create a suspicion (tuhmah) of favoritism or collusion. He argues that these 'places of suspicion' are excluded from the generality of agency, just as such persons are excluded from giving testimony for him. The Sahibayn, however, permit contracting with these individuals at a fair market price, except for one's own slave or mukatib. Their reasoning is that the agency is absolute (unrestricted) and there is no inherent suspicion because the properties and benefits are separate—the agent does not gain personally except in the case of the slave (where the slave's property belongs to the master) and the mukatib (where the master has a potential interest). This difference also applies to contracts of ijarah (hire) and sarf (currency exchange). The commentary further mentions that Sahib al-Quduri clarifies this position, and it aligns with one view of Imam al-Shafi'i and, in some narrations, Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad. The passage demonstrates how Hanafi jurisprudence balances the general permissibility of agency with exceptions based on potential conflict of interest.

Key Terms
تہمت (tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or bias that may affect the fairness of the transaction.
موضع تہمت (mawdi' tuhmah): A situation or relationship that gives rise to suspicion; here, it refers to transactions with close relatives.
مکاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah (manumission by payments) with his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, with whom among the following is it NOT permissible for the agent to contract?
A) The agent's father
B) The agent's grandfather
C) The agent's own slave
D) The agent's wife

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between general permissibility in agency and specific prohibitions based on tuhmah, demonstrating the importance of conflict of interest in Islamic commercial law.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Absolute Agency and Dealing with Relatives: The Dispute of Tuhma
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency and tuhma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding an agent's authority to contract with close relatives and evaluate the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
An absolute agency does not automatically grant authority to deal with relatives where tuhma exists.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of an absolute agency. The key question: may an agent sell or buy from his own close relatives? The answer reveals the Hanafi legal concept of tuhma (suspicion) and its impact on contractual authority. Understanding this dispute helps you grasp how the Hanafi school balances textual generality with practical considerations of bias.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage asks whether a wakil with absolute authority (not restricted to specific persons) can contract with his father, grandfather, and those whose testimony is not accepted in his favor (e.g., son, wife).
The issue revolves around 'mawadi' al-tuhma' (places of suspicion).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that such contracts are invalid (batil). His proof: tuhma is established because these relatives cannot testify for the agent, indicating suspicion, and the benefits between them are connected (ittisal al-manafi'), making it akin to selling to oneself.
He cites the non-acceptance of testimony as a clear indicator of tuhma.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it, provided the transaction is at fair market price (mithl al-qima), with the sole exception of the agent's own slave or mukatab. Their proof: the agency is absolute, and there is no tuhma because properties (amlak) are separate and benefits (manafi') are disconnected.
They argue that if there were tuhma, the agent could not even contract with strangers, but that is clearly allowed.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the contract is void (batil) and must be rescinded. Under the Sahibayn, it is valid if at fair price; if the price is below market, it may be invalid due to loss to the principal.
Later commentaries discuss whether a slight loss (nain bair) is tolerated; some say yes, others no.
5
Note the scope of the dispute
This dispute applies only when the agency is absolute (mutlaq). If the principal says 'sell to whomever you wish' (amum al-mashiyyah), all agree the agent can deal with relatives.
The exception of slave/mukatab is agreed upon because the agent has direct ownership interest.
