Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Agent's Return of Defective Goods to Principal via Qadi's Decree: No Separate Suit Needed
Explains when agent can return defective slave to principal without court proceeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios that allow an agent to return a defective slave to the principal without separate litigation, and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) behind the rule.

Core Idea
A qadi's decree returning a defective slave to the agent is itself a return to the principal – no separate lawsuit needed.

Imagine you appoint an agent to sell your slave. The buyer later discovers a hidden defect and goes to the qadi, who orders the slave returned to your agent. Can your agent simply hand the slave back to you, or must she drag you to court? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: the qadi's decree itself transfers the slave back to you. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal act being ruled upon? The agent returning a defective slave to the principal after the buyer obtained a qadi's decree. The ruling (hukm) is that this return is valid and legally effective without separate litigation.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof supports the ruling? The dalil is the qadi's certainty ('ilm) that the defect existed with the seller (principal). The passage states: the qadi is fully certain that the defect could not have arisen in the buyer's possession, so the decree returns the slave to the seller (principal) directly.
This is a qiyas-based proof: the qadi's knowledge substitutes for formal evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? Because the qadi's certainty that the defect existed pre-sale makes the decree equivalent to the seller's own acknowledgment; thus the agent's return to the principal is automatic.
The logical link: qadi's knowledge → certainty of pre-existing defect → decree returns to seller → agent need not litigate again.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Do other schools differ? Yes, the Hanafi position is that no separate suit is needed; other schools may require the agent to initiate a new case against the principal. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different views on the effect of a qadi's decree.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is operative? The Hanafi position, as recorded in zahir al-riwayah (al-Jami' al-Saghir), is the mufta bihi. It is based on the strong analogy that the qadi's decree is conclusive proof that the defect was with the principal.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah confirms this, and the commentator (Ashraf al-Hidayah) expands without contradiction.

كتاب الوكالة

ہے۔ اسلئے کہ قاضی کو بائع کے قبضہ میں عیب پیدا ہونے کا یقین ہے۔ لہذا قاضی کا فیصلہ ان حجتوں کی طرف منسوب نہ ہوگا اور کتاب میں ان حجتوں کے شرط لگانے کی تاویل یہ ہے کہ قاضی کو علم ہے کہ اس طرح کا عیب مثلاً ایک ماہ کی مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے لیکن قاضی پر بیچ کی تاریخ مشتبہ ہو گئی تو وہ تاریخ ظاہر ہونے کے واسطے ان محبتوں کی طرف محتاج ہوگا۔ یا ایسا عیب تھا جس کو صرف عورتیں اور طبیب ہی پہنچانتے ہیں۔ اور عورتوں اور طبیب کا قول جھگڑا متوجہ ہونے میں حجت ہے اور بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے میں حجت نہیں ہے۔ حتی کہ اگر قاضی نے بیچ کا مشاہدہ کیا ہو اور عیب ظاہر ہو تو قاضی ان میں سے کسی حجت کا محتاج نہیں ہو گا ۔ اور یہی موکل پر واپسی ہے۔ پس وکیل واپس کرنے اور خصومت کرنے کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دوسرے شخص کو اپنا غلام فروخت کرنے کا وکیل کیا پس وکیل نے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا اور مشتری کے حوالہ کر دیا، وکیل نے شمن کے قبضہ کیا ہو یا نہ کیا ہو دونوں صورتوں میں اگر مشتری نے اس غلام کو وکیل کی طرف ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس عیب کے مثل مشتری کی قبضہ میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے۔ مثلاً غلام کے ہاتھ میں زائد انگلی کا ہونا، یا ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس کے مثل اس مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے جو مدت مشتری کے قبضہ کی ہے اور مشتری کا اس معیب غلام کو واپس کرنا قاضی کے حکم سے ہوا ہو اور قاضی کا یہ حکم مشتری کے پیش کردہ بینہ کی وجہ سے ہو یا بینہ پیش نہ کرنے کی صورت میں وکیل کے قسم کھانے سے انکار کی وجہ سے ہو۔ یا بدینہ پیش نہ کرنے کی صورت میں وکیل کے قسم کھانے سے انکار کی وجہ سے ہو یا وکیل کے عیب کا اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے ہو تینوں صورتوں میں وکیل اس معیب غلام کو بغیر خصومت کے مؤکل کی طرف واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ یعنی وکیل اس معیب غلام کو مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے خصومت اور قضائے قاضی کا محتاج نہ ہوگا کیونکہ قضائے قاضی کی وجہ سے مشتری کا غلام کو وکیل کی طرف واپس کرنا یہی مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہے ، مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے علیحدہ خصومت اور جھگڑے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ دلیل ... اس کی یہ ہے کہ کلام چونکہ اس عیب کے بارے میں ہے جس کے مثل پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا ہے۔ اسلئے قاضی کو پورے طور پر یقین ہے کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں موجود تھا اور جب قاضی کو بائع کے قبضہ میں مذکورہ عیب کے ہونے کا یقین ہے تو قاضی اپنے علم و یقین کی روشنی میں اس معیب غلام کو بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے اور اس فیصلہ کے تحت غلام کی واپسی جس طرح وکیل کی طرف ہو گی۔ اسی طرح مؤکل کی طرف بھی ہوگی۔

اعتراض لیکن اس پر یہ اعتراض ہو گا کہ جب عیب ایسا ہے جس کے مثل مشتری کے قبضہ میں پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا ہے تو پھر قاضی کے فیصلہ کو مذکورہ جنتوں یعنی بینہ وغیرہ پر کیوں موقوف رکھا گیا ہے۔ قاضی اپنے علم و یقین کی وجہ سے بغیر ان حجبتوں کے بھی فیصلہ صادر کر سکتا ہے۔ جواب صاحب ہدایہ نے اس کا جواب دیتے ہوئے فرمایا فلم يكن قضاؤه مستنداً الى هذه الحجج .... الخ یعنی قاضی کا معیب غلام کو واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ ان حجتوں پر موقوف نہیں ہے، یہاں عبارت میں اگر فلم یکن کی جگہ ولم یکن یعنی فار کی جگہ واو ہوتا تو عبارت زیادہ واضح ہو جاتی۔ سوال لیکن اب یہ سوال پیدا ہو گا کہ جب قاضی کو اس بات کا یقین ہے کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں تھا اور مشتری کے قبضہ میں آکر پیدا نہیں ہوا ہے اور قاضی کا فیصلہ مذکورہ محبتوں پر موقوف نہیں ہے تو متن (جامع صغیر میں ان حجتوں کو ذکر کرنے اور ان کی شرط لگانے سے کیا فائدہ ہے۔

جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کو یہ بات تو معلوم ہے کہ اس طرح کا عیب مثلاً ایک ماہ کی مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا۔ لیکن قاضی بیچ کی تاریخ مشتبہ ہوگئی کہ بیچ کب منعقد ہوئی تھی کیونکہ بیچ اگر دو چار ماہ پہلے منعقد ہوئی ہو تو یہ عیب مشتری کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہوسکتا ہے اور اس عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری غلام کو واپس کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر بیع کو منعقد ہوئے ایک ماہ کا عرصہ بھی نہیں گذرا تو یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہوگا۔ اور مشتری اس عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا۔ پس مشتری بیع کی تاریخ متعین کرنے کیلئے ان تین حجتوں میں سے ایک حجت کا محتاج ہوگا۔ یعنی یا تو مشتری اس بات پر بینہ پیش کرے کہ بیع کے انعقاد پر زیادہ عرصہ نہیں گذرا ہے۔ لہذا یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہے اور یا بائع قسم کھانے سے انکار کرے اور یا بائع (وکیل) اس عیب کا اقرار کرے۔ پس چونکہ بیع کی تاریخ ظاہر کرنے کیلئے ان حجتوں کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے۔ اسلئے جامع صغیر میں ان حجتوں کا شرط ہونا ذکر کر دیا گیا۔

دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ بعض مرتبہ وہ عیب جس کی وجہ سے مشتری مبیع کو واپس کرنے کا ارادہ کرتا ہے ایسا ہوتا ہے جس کو صرف عورتیں ہی جانتی ہیں۔ مثلاً مبیع باندی ہو اور اس کی شرمگاہ میں کوئی بیماری ہونا یا عیب ایسا ہو جس کو صرف طبیب لوگ جانتے ہیں مثلاً خونی کھانسی۔ اور عورتوں اور طبیب کا قول اس بارے میں تو حجت ہو سکتا ہے کہ مشتری اپنے بائع کے ساتھ خصومت اور جھگڑا کرے لیکن عورتوں اور طبیب کے قول کی وجہ سے مبیع بائع کی طرف واپس نہیں کی جاسکتی ہے۔ لہذا مبیع واپس کرنے کیلئے مذکورہ حجتوں کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی۔ اسی وجہ سے جامع صغیر میں مذکورہ حجتوں کا شرط ہونا ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ لیکن اگر قاضی نے بیع کا مشاہدہ کیا یعنی بیع کا انعقاد اس کے سامنے ہوا اور اس کو اس کی تاریخ بھی معلوم ہے اور عیب بالکل ظاہر ہے تو اس صورت میں قاضی مذکورہ حجتوں میں سے کسی حجت کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ کسی حجت کے بغیر غلام واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ صادر کر دے گا۔ اور قاضی کے اس فیصلہ کی وجہ سے مشتری کا وکیل کی طرف غلام واپس کرنا یہی مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہوگا۔ یعنی وکیل اپنے موکل کے ساتھ نہ کسی خصومت کا محتاج ہوگا اور نہ غلام کو موکل کی طرف پس کرنے ہی سے غلام مؤکل کی طرف واپس ہو جائے گا۔

اعتراض یہاں یہ اعتراض ہو سکتا ہے کہ جب وکیل نے عیب کا اقرار کیا تو اس صورت میں قضائے قاضی کی کیا ضرورت ہے۔ اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے وکیل معیب غلام کو قبول کر ہی لے گا۔ حالانکہ متن میں اقرار کے ساتھ بھی قضائے قاضی کا ذکر کیا ہے۔ یعنی اقرار کی صورت میں بھی قاضی کا فیصلہ کرنا ضروری ہے۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایسا ہو سکتا ہے کہ وکیل عیب کا اقرار تو کرلے لیکن اس کے بعد وہ معیب غلام کو قبول کرنے سے رک جائے تو اس صورت میں قاضی کا فیصلہ اس کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کرنا ہوگا۔ یعنی اس صورت میں وکیل کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کرنے کیلئے قاضی کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی۔

جس عیب کی مثل پیدا ہو سکتا ہے اس کا بھی یہی حکم ہے: وكذلك ان رده عليه بعيب يحدث مثله ببينة او باباء يمين لان البيئة حجة مطلقة والوكيل مضطر في النكول لبعد العيب عن علمه باعتبار عدم ممارسة المبيع فلزم الأمر
Book of Agency. ... Because the judge is certain that the defect occurred while in the possession of the seller. Therefore the judge's decision will not be attributed to these proofs, and the interpretation of stipulating these proofs in the book is that the judge knows that such a defect cannot arise in a period of one month, but the date of sale became ambiguous to the judge, so he will need these proofs to clarify the date. Or it was a defect that only women and physicians recognize. And the statement of women and physicians is evidence for initiating litigation but not for returning to the seller. Even if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect is apparent, the judge will not need any of these proofs. And that itself is a return to the principal. Thus the agent will not need to return and litigate. Explanation: The scenario is that one person appointed another as agent to sell his slave; the agent sold the slave and handed him over to the buyer, whether the agent took possession of the price or not. In both cases, if the buyer returned the slave to the agent due to a defect whose like cannot arise in the buyer's possession – e.g., an extra finger on the slave's hand – or returned due to a defect whose like cannot arise within the period the buyer possessed it, and the buyer's return of the defective slave occurred by the judge's order, and the judge's order was due to evidence presented by the buyer, or due to the agent's refusal to take an oath when no evidence was presented, or due to the agent's acknowledgment of the defect – in all three cases the agent can return the defective slave to the principal without litigation. The agent does not need litigation and a judge's decree to return the defective slave to the principal, because the buyer's return of the slave to the agent by the judge's decree is itself a return to the principal; there is no need for separate litigation and dispute for returning to the principal. Proof: This is because the speech concerns a defect whose like cannot arise, so the judge is fully certain that this defect existed in the seller's possession. When the judge is certain that the mentioned defect existed in the seller's possession, he can decide to return the defective slave to the seller based on his knowledge and certainty, and under that decision the return of the slave to the agent will also be a return to the principal. Objection: But it will be objected that when the defect is such that its like cannot arise in the buyer's possession, why is the judge's decision made conditional upon the mentioned proofs? The judge can issue a decision based on his own knowledge and certainty without these proofs. Answer: The author of al-Hidayah answered: 'فلم يكن قضاؤه مستنداً الى هذه الحجج' i.e., the judge's decision to return the defective slave is not dependent on these proofs. The phrase uses 'فلم يكن' and if it were 'ولم يكن' (with waw instead of fa) it would be clearer. Question: But now a question arises: When the judge is certain that this defect was in the seller's possession and did not arise after coming into the buyer's possession, and the judge's decision is not dependent on these proofs, what is the benefit of mentioning and stipulating these proofs in the text (al-Jami' al-Saghir)? Answer: The answer is that the judge knows that such a defect cannot arise in e.g. one month, but the date of the sale became ambiguous – when the sale was concluded. If the sale occurred two or four months earlier, this defect could have arisen in the buyer's possession, and the buyer would not be permitted to return the slave due to this defect. But if not even one month has passed since the sale, then this defect would have existed in the seller's possession, and the buyer is permitted to return the slave due to this defect. Therefore, to determine the date of sale, the buyer needs one of three proofs: either he presents evidence that not much time has passed since the sale, so the defect existed in the seller's possession; or the seller refuses to take an oath; or the seller (agent) acknowledges the defect. Since these proofs are needed to clarify the date of sale, the stipulation of these proofs was mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. Second answer: Sometimes the defect due to which the buyer intends to return the sold item is such that only women know it – e.g., if the sold item is a slave-girl and she has a disease in her private part – or it is a defect that only physicians know, e.g., bloody cough. The statement of women and physicians can be evidence for the buyer to initiate litigation with the seller, but the sold item cannot be returned to the seller based on their statement alone. Therefore, to return the sold item, the need for the mentioned proofs arises. Hence the stipulation of these proofs was mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. However, if the judge witnessed the sale – the sale took place in his presence and he also knows its date – and the defect is completely apparent, then the judge does not need any of these proofs. Rather, he will issue a decision to return the slave without any proof, and the buyer's return of the slave to the agent by the judge's decision will itself be a return to the principal. That is, the agent does not need any litigation with his principal, nor does the agent need to hand over the slave to the principal; the slave will be returned to the principal automatically. Objection: Here it could be objected that when the agent acknowledged the defect, what need is there for the judge's decree? Due to acknowledgment, the agent will accept the defective slave anyway. Yet the matn mentions the judge's decree along with acknowledgment – i.e., even in the case of acknowledgment, the judge's decision is necessary. Answer: The answer is that it is possible that the agent acknowledges the defect but then refrains from accepting the defective slave. In that case, the judge's decision would compel him to accept. So in this case, the need for the judge arises to force the agent to accept. The same ruling applies to a defect whose like can arise: likewise if the buyer returns it to him due to a defect whose like can arise, by evidence or refusal to take an oath, because evidence is an absolute proof and the agent is compelled by the refusal because the defect is beyond his knowledge due to lack of familiarity with the sold item, so the matter becomes binding.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah expands on a ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding the agency in sale and return of defective goods. The central rule: when a buyer obtains a judge's order to return a defective slave to the agent (wakil), that order itself constitutes a return to the principal (muwakkil). The agent need not initiate separate litigation against the principal to pass the slave back. The reason is that the judge's decree is based on certainty that the defect existed with the seller (principal), so the return is effectively to the principal.

The passage discusses three scenarios that trigger the judge's decree: (1) the buyer presents evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed before purchase, (2) the agent refuses to take an oath (when the buyer has no evidence), or (3) the agent acknowledges the defect. In all three, the agent can return the item to the principal without additional court proceedings.

An objection arises: if the judge is certain the defect existed with the seller, why does the text in al-Jami' al-Saghir stipulate the need for these proofs? The author gives two answers. First, the judge may know the defect cannot arise in a specific time (e.g., one month), but he does not know exactly when the sale occurred. The proofs are needed to establish the sale's date, because if the sale was long ago, the defect could have arisen after purchase. Second, some defects are known only to women or physicians; their testimony is accepted to start litigation but not to compel a return. The proofs overcome this limitation.

However, if the judge personally witnessed the sale and knows its date, and the defect is obvious, no proofs are needed; he can decide based on his own knowledge. That decision also suffices as return to the principal.

A further objection: if the agent acknowledges the defect, why is a judge's decree needed? Because the agent might acknowledge but then refuse to accept the slave back; the decree then forces compliance.

The same ruling applies to defects that could arise after purchase: the buyer can return with evidence or the agent's refusal to swear, because evidence is absolute and the agent's lack of knowledge due to unfamiliarity with the sold item justifies compulsion.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to sell a slave on behalf of the principal (muwakkil).
موكل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints the agent; the owner of the slave being sold.
مشتری (mushtarī): Buyer who purchases the slave from the agent and later returns it due to a defect.
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in the slave that diminishes value or utility, unknown to the buyer at the time of sale.
قاضی (qāḍī): Judge who issues a decree (qada') returning the defective slave from buyer to agent.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.
قسم (qasam): Oath sworn by the agent (or seller) when the buyer has no evidence; refusal (aba') implies acceptance of the defect's pre-existence.
اقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment by the agent that the defect existed before sale; this is binding and triggers the return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the discussed Hanafi ruling, if the buyer returns a defective slave to the agent by a qadi's decree based on the agent's refusal to take an oath, what is the agent's obligation regarding returning the slave to the principal?
A) The agent must file a new lawsuit against the principal to return the slave.
B) The agent can return the slave to the principal without any further litigation.
C) The agent must first accept the slave and then sell it again to return the price.
D) The agent must obtain the principal's consent before returning the slave.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer returns a defective item to an agent by a qadi's decree, that decree suffices to return the item to the principal; the agent need not file a separate suit.

Why Proofs are Stipulated Despite Qadi's Certainty: Date Ambiguity and Special Defects
Explains why proofs are required despite qadi's certainty, and when they are not needed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons why the text stipulates proofs (evidence, oath refusal, acknowledgment) for the qadi's decree, and identify when the qadi can dispense with proofs.

Core Idea
Proofs are needed to clarify the sale date or to establish defects known only to specialists, but not when the qadi himself witnessed the sale.

If the qadi is so certain the defect was pre-existing, why does al-Jami' al-Saghir mention that his decree must be based on evidence, oath refusal, or acknowledgment? This objection is answered in two ways: first, the qadi may not know the exact date of sale; second, some defects are known only to women or physicians. But if the qadi witnessed the sale and the defect is obvious, no proofs are needed. This lesson unpacks those logical defenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base text claim and the objection
The base text (al-Jami' al-Saghir) stipulates that the qadi's decree must be based on evidence, oath refusal, or acknowledgment. The objection is: if the qadi is certain the defect existed with the seller, why does he need these proofs?
The objection is a logical challenge to the text's consistency.
2
Analyze the first answer: date ambiguity
The qadi knows the defect cannot arise in, say, one month, but he does not know the sale date. If the sale occurred long ago, the defect could have arisen after purchase. The proofs (evidence, oath refusal, acknowledgment) are needed to establish the sale date, not the defect's nature.
This answer preserves the qadi's certainty about the defect while acknowledging informational gaps.
3
Analyze the second answer: defects known only to specialists
Some defects are only recognized by women (e.g., diseases of a slave-girl's private parts) or physicians. Their testimony is accepted to start litigation but not to compel a return. Therefore, the stipulated proofs (evidence, oath, acknowledgment) are needed to provide the missing evidentiary link.
This answer deals with the limitation of certain testimonies in Hanafi procedure.
4
Identify the exception: qadi witnessed the sale
If the qadi himself witnessed the sale and knows its date, and the defect is apparent, then no proofs are needed. He can issue the decree based on his own knowledge.
This exception confirms that the proofs are only needed when the qadi lacks personal knowledge of the sale.
5
Note the extension to defects that can arise
The same ruling (agent's return without separate suit) also applies to defects that could arise after purchase, if the buyer provides evidence or the agent refuses the oath, because evidence is absolute and the agent's lack of familiarity compels acceptance.
This extends the ruling beyond the initial scenario of defects that cannot arise.

Source Text

كتاب الوكالة

ہے۔ اسلئے کہ قاضی کو بائع کے قبضہ میں عیب پیدا ہونے کا یقین ہے۔ لہذا قاضی کا فیصلہ ان حجتوں کی طرف منسوب نہ ہوگا اور کتاب میں ان حجتوں کے شرط لگانے کی تاویل یہ ہے کہ قاضی کو علم ہے کہ اس طرح کا عیب مثلاً ایک ماہ کی مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے لیکن قاضی پر بیچ کی تاریخ مشتبہ ہو گئی تو وہ تاریخ ظاہر ہونے کے واسطے ان محبتوں کی طرف محتاج ہوگا۔ یا ایسا عیب تھا جس کو صرف عورتیں اور طبیب ہی پہنچانتے ہیں۔ اور عورتوں اور طبیب کا قول جھگڑا متوجہ ہونے میں حجت ہے اور بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے میں حجت نہیں ہے۔ حتی کہ اگر قاضی نے بیچ کا مشاہدہ کیا ہو اور عیب ظاہر ہو تو قاضی ان میں سے کسی حجت کا محتاج نہیں ہو گا ۔ اور یہی موکل پر واپسی ہے۔ پس وکیل واپس کرنے اور خصومت کرنے کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے دوسرے شخص کو اپنا غلام فروخت کرنے کا وکیل کیا پس وکیل نے اس غلام کو فروخت کر دیا اور مشتری کے حوالہ کر دیا، وکیل نے شمن کے قبضہ کیا ہو یا نہ کیا ہو دونوں صورتوں میں اگر مشتری نے اس غلام کو وکیل کی طرف ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس عیب کے مثل مشتری کی قبضہ میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے۔ مثلاً غلام کے ہاتھ میں زائد انگلی کا ہونا، یا ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس کے مثل اس مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا ہے جو مدت مشتری کے قبضہ کی ہے اور مشتری کا اس معیب غلام کو واپس کرنا قاضی کے حکم سے ہوا ہو اور قاضی کا یہ حکم مشتری کے پیش کردہ بینہ کی وجہ سے ہو یا بینہ پیش نہ کرنے کی صورت میں وکیل کے قسم کھانے سے انکار کی وجہ سے ہو۔ یا بدینہ پیش نہ کرنے کی صورت میں وکیل کے قسم کھانے سے انکار کی وجہ سے ہو یا وکیل کے عیب کا اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے ہو تینوں صورتوں میں وکیل اس معیب غلام کو بغیر خصومت کے مؤکل کی طرف واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ یعنی وکیل اس معیب غلام کو مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے خصومت اور قضائے قاضی کا محتاج نہ ہوگا کیونکہ قضائے قاضی کی وجہ سے مشتری کا غلام کو وکیل کی طرف واپس کرنا یہی مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہے ، مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے علیحدہ خصومت اور جھگڑے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ دلیل ... اس کی یہ ہے کہ کلام چونکہ اس عیب کے بارے میں ہے جس کے مثل پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا ہے۔ اسلئے قاضی کو پورے طور پر یقین ہے کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں موجود تھا اور جب قاضی کو بائع کے قبضہ میں مذکورہ عیب کے ہونے کا یقین ہے تو قاضی اپنے علم و یقین کی روشنی میں اس معیب غلام کو بائع کی طرف واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ کر سکتا ہے اور اس فیصلہ کے تحت غلام کی واپسی جس طرح وکیل کی طرف ہو گی۔ اسی طرح مؤکل کی طرف بھی ہوگی۔

اعتراض لیکن اس پر یہ اعتراض ہو گا کہ جب عیب ایسا ہے جس کے مثل مشتری کے قبضہ میں پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا ہے تو پھر قاضی کے فیصلہ کو مذکورہ جنتوں یعنی بینہ وغیرہ پر کیوں موقوف رکھا گیا ہے۔ قاضی اپنے علم و یقین کی وجہ سے بغیر ان حجبتوں کے بھی فیصلہ صادر کر سکتا ہے۔ جواب صاحب ہدایہ نے اس کا جواب دیتے ہوئے فرمایا فلم يكن قضاؤه مستنداً الى هذه الحجج .... الخ یعنی قاضی کا معیب غلام کو واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ ان حجتوں پر موقوف نہیں ہے، یہاں عبارت میں اگر فلم یکن کی جگہ ولم یکن یعنی فار کی جگہ واو ہوتا تو عبارت زیادہ واضح ہو جاتی۔ سوال لیکن اب یہ سوال پیدا ہو گا کہ جب قاضی کو اس بات کا یقین ہے کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں تھا اور مشتری کے قبضہ میں آکر پیدا نہیں ہوا ہے اور قاضی کا فیصلہ مذکورہ محبتوں پر موقوف نہیں ہے تو متن (جامع صغیر میں ان حجتوں کو ذکر کرنے اور ان کی شرط لگانے سے کیا فائدہ ہے۔

جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کو یہ بات تو معلوم ہے کہ اس طرح کا عیب مثلاً ایک ماہ کی مدت میں پیدا نہیں ہوسکتا۔ لیکن قاضی بیچ کی تاریخ مشتبہ ہوگئی کہ بیچ کب منعقد ہوئی تھی کیونکہ بیچ اگر دو چار ماہ پہلے منعقد ہوئی ہو تو یہ عیب مشتری کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہوسکتا ہے اور اس عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری غلام کو واپس کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر بیع کو منعقد ہوئے ایک ماہ کا عرصہ بھی نہیں گذرا تو یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہوگا۔ اور مشتری اس عیب کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا۔ پس مشتری بیع کی تاریخ متعین کرنے کیلئے ان تین حجتوں میں سے ایک حجت کا محتاج ہوگا۔ یعنی یا تو مشتری اس بات پر بینہ پیش کرے کہ بیع کے انعقاد پر زیادہ عرصہ نہیں گذرا ہے۔ لہذا یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ میں پیدا شدہ ہے اور یا بائع قسم کھانے سے انکار کرے اور یا بائع (وکیل) اس عیب کا اقرار کرے۔ پس چونکہ بیع کی تاریخ ظاہر کرنے کیلئے ان حجتوں کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے۔ اسلئے جامع صغیر میں ان حجتوں کا شرط ہونا ذکر کر دیا گیا۔

دوسرا جواب یہ ہے کہ بعض مرتبہ وہ عیب جس کی وجہ سے مشتری مبیع کو واپس کرنے کا ارادہ کرتا ہے ایسا ہوتا ہے جس کو صرف عورتیں ہی جانتی ہیں۔ مثلاً مبیع باندی ہو اور اس کی شرمگاہ میں کوئی بیماری ہونا یا عیب ایسا ہو جس کو صرف طبیب لوگ جانتے ہیں مثلاً خونی کھانسی۔ اور عورتوں اور طبیب کا قول اس بارے میں تو حجت ہو سکتا ہے کہ مشتری اپنے بائع کے ساتھ خصومت اور جھگڑا کرے لیکن عورتوں اور طبیب کے قول کی وجہ سے مبیع بائع کی طرف واپس نہیں کی جاسکتی ہے۔ لہذا مبیع واپس کرنے کیلئے مذکورہ حجتوں کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی۔ اسی وجہ سے جامع صغیر میں مذکورہ حجتوں کا شرط ہونا ذکر کیا گیا ہے۔ لیکن اگر قاضی نے بیع کا مشاہدہ کیا یعنی بیع کا انعقاد اس کے سامنے ہوا اور اس کو اس کی تاریخ بھی معلوم ہے اور عیب بالکل ظاہر ہے تو اس صورت میں قاضی مذکورہ حجتوں میں سے کسی حجت کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ کسی حجت کے بغیر غلام واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ صادر کر دے گا۔ اور قاضی کے اس فیصلہ کی وجہ سے مشتری کا وکیل کی طرف غلام واپس کرنا یہی مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنا ہوگا۔ یعنی وکیل اپنے موکل کے ساتھ نہ کسی خصومت کا محتاج ہوگا اور نہ غلام کو موکل کی طرف پس کرنے ہی سے غلام مؤکل کی طرف واپس ہو جائے گا۔

اعتراض یہاں یہ اعتراض ہو سکتا ہے کہ جب وکیل نے عیب کا اقرار کیا تو اس صورت میں قضائے قاضی کی کیا ضرورت ہے۔ اقرار کرنے کی وجہ سے وکیل معیب غلام کو قبول کر ہی لے گا۔ حالانکہ متن میں اقرار کے ساتھ بھی قضائے قاضی کا ذکر کیا ہے۔ یعنی اقرار کی صورت میں بھی قاضی کا فیصلہ کرنا ضروری ہے۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ایسا ہو سکتا ہے کہ وکیل عیب کا اقرار تو کرلے لیکن اس کے بعد وہ معیب غلام کو قبول کرنے سے رک جائے تو اس صورت میں قاضی کا فیصلہ اس کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کرنا ہوگا۔ یعنی اس صورت میں وکیل کو قبول کرنے پر مجبور کرنے کیلئے قاضی کی ضرورت پیش آئے گی۔

جس عیب کی مثل پیدا ہو سکتا ہے اس کا بھی یہی حکم ہے: وكذلك ان رده عليه بعيب يحدث مثله ببينة او باباء يمين لان البيئة حجة مطلقة والوكيل مضطر في النكول لبعد العيب عن علمه باعتبار عدم ممارسة المبيع فلزم الأمر
Book of Agency. ... Because the judge is certain that the defect occurred while in the possession of the seller. Therefore the judge's decision will not be attributed to these proofs, and the interpretation of stipulating these proofs in the book is that the judge knows that such a defect cannot arise in a period of one month, but the date of sale became ambiguous to the judge, so he will need these proofs to clarify the date. Or it was a defect that only women and physicians recognize. And the statement of women and physicians is evidence for initiating litigation but not for returning to the seller. Even if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect is apparent, the judge will not need any of these proofs. And that itself is a return to the principal. Thus the agent will not need to return and litigate. Explanation: The scenario is that one person appointed another as agent to sell his slave; the agent sold the slave and handed him over to the buyer, whether the agent took possession of the price or not. In both cases, if the buyer returned the slave to the agent due to a defect whose like cannot arise in the buyer's possession – e.g., an extra finger on the slave's hand – or returned due to a defect whose like cannot arise within the period the buyer possessed it, and the buyer's return of the defective slave occurred by the judge's order, and the judge's order was due to evidence presented by the buyer, or due to the agent's refusal to take an oath when no evidence was presented, or due to the agent's acknowledgment of the defect – in all three cases the agent can return the defective slave to the principal without litigation. The agent does not need litigation and a judge's decree to return the defective slave to the principal, because the buyer's return of the slave to the agent by the judge's decree is itself a return to the principal; there is no need for separate litigation and dispute for returning to the principal. Proof: This is because the speech concerns a defect whose like cannot arise, so the judge is fully certain that this defect existed in the seller's possession. When the judge is certain that the mentioned defect existed in the seller's possession, he can decide to return the defective slave to the seller based on his knowledge and certainty, and under that decision the return of the slave to the agent will also be a return to the principal. Objection: But it will be objected that when the defect is such that its like cannot arise in the buyer's possession, why is the judge's decision made conditional upon the mentioned proofs? The judge can issue a decision based on his own knowledge and certainty without these proofs. Answer: The author of al-Hidayah answered: 'فلم يكن قضاؤه مستنداً الى هذه الحجج' i.e., the judge's decision to return the defective slave is not dependent on these proofs. The phrase uses 'فلم يكن' and if it were 'ولم يكن' (with waw instead of fa) it would be clearer. Question: But now a question arises: When the judge is certain that this defect was in the seller's possession and did not arise after coming into the buyer's possession, and the judge's decision is not dependent on these proofs, what is the benefit of mentioning and stipulating these proofs in the text (al-Jami' al-Saghir)? Answer: The answer is that the judge knows that such a defect cannot arise in e.g. one month, but the date of the sale became ambiguous – when the sale was concluded. If the sale occurred two or four months earlier, this defect could have arisen in the buyer's possession, and the buyer would not be permitted to return the slave due to this defect. But if not even one month has passed since the sale, then this defect would have existed in the seller's possession, and the buyer is permitted to return the slave due to this defect. Therefore, to determine the date of sale, the buyer needs one of three proofs: either he presents evidence that not much time has passed since the sale, so the defect existed in the seller's possession; or the seller refuses to take an oath; or the seller (agent) acknowledges the defect. Since these proofs are needed to clarify the date of sale, the stipulation of these proofs was mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. Second answer: Sometimes the defect due to which the buyer intends to return the sold item is such that only women know it – e.g., if the sold item is a slave-girl and she has a disease in her private part – or it is a defect that only physicians know, e.g., bloody cough. The statement of women and physicians can be evidence for the buyer to initiate litigation with the seller, but the sold item cannot be returned to the seller based on their statement alone. Therefore, to return the sold item, the need for the mentioned proofs arises. Hence the stipulation of these proofs was mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir. However, if the judge witnessed the sale – the sale took place in his presence and he also knows its date – and the defect is completely apparent, then the judge does not need any of these proofs. Rather, he will issue a decision to return the slave without any proof, and the buyer's return of the slave to the agent by the judge's decision will itself be a return to the principal. That is, the agent does not need any litigation with his principal, nor does the agent need to hand over the slave to the principal; the slave will be returned to the principal automatically. Objection: Here it could be objected that when the agent acknowledged the defect, what need is there for the judge's decree? Due to acknowledgment, the agent will accept the defective slave anyway. Yet the matn mentions the judge's decree along with acknowledgment – i.e., even in the case of acknowledgment, the judge's decision is necessary. Answer: The answer is that it is possible that the agent acknowledges the defect but then refrains from accepting the defective slave. In that case, the judge's decision would compel him to accept. So in this case, the need for the judge arises to force the agent to accept. The same ruling applies to a defect whose like can arise: likewise if the buyer returns it to him due to a defect whose like can arise, by evidence or refusal to take an oath, because evidence is an absolute proof and the agent is compelled by the refusal because the defect is beyond his knowledge due to lack of familiarity with the sold item, so the matter becomes binding.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah expands on a ruling from al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding the agency in sale and return of defective goods. The central rule: when a buyer obtains a judge's order to return a defective slave to the agent (wakil), that order itself constitutes a return to the principal (muwakkil). The agent need not initiate separate litigation against the principal to pass the slave back. The reason is that the judge's decree is based on certainty that the defect existed with the seller (principal), so the return is effectively to the principal.

The passage discusses three scenarios that trigger the judge's decree: (1) the buyer presents evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed before purchase, (2) the agent refuses to take an oath (when the buyer has no evidence), or (3) the agent acknowledges the defect. In all three, the agent can return the item to the principal without additional court proceedings.

An objection arises: if the judge is certain the defect existed with the seller, why does the text in al-Jami' al-Saghir stipulate the need for these proofs? The author gives two answers. First, the judge may know the defect cannot arise in a specific time (e.g., one month), but he does not know exactly when the sale occurred. The proofs are needed to establish the sale's date, because if the sale was long ago, the defect could have arisen after purchase. Second, some defects are known only to women or physicians; their testimony is accepted to start litigation but not to compel a return. The proofs overcome this limitation.

However, if the judge personally witnessed the sale and knows its date, and the defect is obvious, no proofs are needed; he can decide based on his own knowledge. That decision also suffices as return to the principal.

A further objection: if the agent acknowledges the defect, why is a judge's decree needed? Because the agent might acknowledge but then refuse to accept the slave back; the decree then forces compliance.

The same ruling applies to defects that could arise after purchase: the buyer can return with evidence or the agent's refusal to swear, because evidence is absolute and the agent's lack of knowledge due to unfamiliarity with the sold item justifies compulsion.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): Agent appointed to sell a slave on behalf of the principal (muwakkil).
موكل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints the agent; the owner of the slave being sold.
مشتری (mushtarī): Buyer who purchases the slave from the agent and later returns it due to a defect.
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in the slave that diminishes value or utility, unknown to the buyer at the time of sale.
قاضی (qāḍī): Judge who issues a decree (qada') returning the defective slave from buyer to agent.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.
قسم (qasam): Oath sworn by the agent (or seller) when the buyer has no evidence; refusal (aba') implies acceptance of the defect's pre-existence.
اقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment by the agent that the defect existed before sale; this is binding and triggers the return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the passage, why might proofs be needed for a return based on a defect that only women know?
A) Because women's testimony cannot be used in court at all.
B) Because women's testimony is only valid for initiating litigation, not for compelling a return.
C) Because the qadi does not trust women witnesses.
D) Because the agent must always acknowledge the defect.

Key Takeaway
Proofs are stipulated not because the qadi doubts the defect's pre-existence, but to resolve sale date ambiguity or to supplement testimonies of specialists.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Return of Defective Goods to Agent: Binding the Principal via Evidence or Refusal of Oath
Ruling with proof on agency and defect return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two ways a buyer can return a defective good to an agent such that the liability falls on the principal, and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind each.

Core Idea
Return to agent binds principal if buyer provides evidence or agent refuses oath.

In Hanafi sales law, an agent (wakil) acts on behalf of the principal (muwakkil). When a defect is discovered after purchase, the buyer may return the item to the agent. But the legal effect depends on the proof. This passage explains that if the buyer presents conclusive evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed before the sale, or if the agent refuses to swear an oath (nukul) claiming ignorance, the good is deemed returned to the principal—not to the agent personally. This is because evidence is a complete proof, and the agent's refusal indicates he was not familiar with the item due to the principal's failure to inform him. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the buyer returning a defective good to the agent. The ruling is that the return binds the principal (muwakkil) if the defect is of a type that could have arisen later and the return is supported by evidence (bayyinah) or the agent's refusal of the oath (nukul).
The defect is 'ayb yahdathu mithluh'—a defect that could possibly have arisen after the sale.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text states: 'لان البينة حجة مطلقة والوكيل مضطر في النكول لبعد العيب عن علمه' (because evidence is a complete proof, and the agent is compelled to refuse the oath because the defect is far from his knowledge). This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of evidence and the agent's position.
The proof is not a Quranic verse or hadith but a legal maxim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
Bayyinah is a complete proof; it establishes the fact of the defect's existence at the time of the seller's possession conclusively. Since the agent represents the principal, this fact is imputed to the principal. For nukul, the agent's refusal indicates he cannot truthfully deny knowledge; his ignorance is due to the principal's failure to inform him, so the liability reverts to the principal.
The theory of imputation: the agent's actions (or omissions) bind the principal within the scope of agency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (dispute)
Although the text presents this as the Hanafi position, there is cross-madhhab dispute. The Shafi'i school, for instance, may hold that the agent's refusal of oath only binds the agent personally, not the principal, because the principal is not a party to the litigation.
The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the principal is responsible for the agent's lack of knowledge.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (relied-upon position)
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the return is to the principal in both cases. This is based on the strength of the evidence (bayyinah as hujja mutlaqa) and the analogy that the agent's position is like that of a party who cannot deny.
The text does not cite a specific tarjih authority but presents it as the established ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ال و کذالک ان رده علیه بعيب يحدث مثله ببينة او باباء يمين لان البينة حجة مطلقة والوكيل مضطر في النكول لبعد العيب عن علمه باعتبار عدم ممارسة المبيع فلزم الأمر. شرح: یعنی اور اسی طرح اگر مشتری نے غلام کو وکیل پر ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس کے مثل پیدا ہو سکتا ہے۔ بینہ کی وجہ سے یا انکار قسم کی وجہ سے۔ اسلئے کہ بینہ حجت کاملہ ہے۔ اور وکیل قسم سے انکار کرنے میں مجبور ہے کیونکہ مبیع کے ساتھ عدم ممارست کی وجہ سے عیب وکیل کے علم سے دور ہے۔ لہذا غلام مؤکل پر لازم ہوگا۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے وکیل کی طرف غلام کو ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس کے مثل پیدا ہو سکتا ہے اور غلام واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ بینہ کی وجہ سے ہوا ہو۔ یا وکیل کے قسم سے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے ہوا ہو ۔ دونوں صورتوں میں یہی واپسی مؤکل کی طرف ہوگی۔ مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے وکیل خصومت کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے اس بات پر بینہ قائم کیا کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ کے وقت بھی موجود تھا۔ اور قاضی نے اس بینہ کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا حکم دیا تو یہ واپسی موکل پر ہو گی۔ اس لئے کہ بینہ حجت کاملہ ہوتا ہے۔ پس بینہ کی وجہ سے عیب کا قیام مؤکل کے پاس ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ اور جب عیب کا قیام مؤکل کے پاس ثابت ہو گیا تو غلام کی واپسی بھی مؤکل ہی کی طرف ہوگی اور وکیل کے انکار عن الیمین کرنے کی صورت میں واپسی مؤکل کی طرف اسلئے ہوگی کہ وکیل قسم سے انکار کرنے پر مجبور ہے اور مجبور اسلئے ہے کہ اس وکیل کو مبیع یعنی غلام کے احوال سے زیادہ سابقہ نہیں پڑا ہے۔ لہذا اس کے عیوب سے بھی زیادہ واقف نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب وکیل غلام کے عیوب سے زیادہ واقف نہیں ہے تو وہ جھوٹی قسم کھانے سے ڈرے گا اور اس کے نتیجہ میں وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دے گا اور وکیل کو اس مصیبت میں موکل ہی نے ڈالا ہے۔ لہذا اس کو چھٹکارا دلانا بھی موکل پر واجب ہوگا یعنی وکیل پر جو ذمہ داری عائد ہوگی وہ موکل کی طرف لوٹ جائے گی۔ پس انکار عن الیمین کا حکم موکل پر لازم ہوگا۔ یعنی انکار عن الیمین کی وجہ سے غلام کی واپسی مؤکل پر ہوگی ۔ اور وکیل کو غلام واپس کرنے کیلئے مؤکل سے خصومت کرنے کی چنداں ضرورت نہ ہوگی۔
And similarly, if he returned it to him due to a defect that could arise later, by evidence or by refusal of oath, because evidence is a complete proof, and the agent is compelled to refuse the oath due to the defect being far from his knowledge because of his lack of dealing with the sold item, so the matter is binding. Explanation: That is, if the buyer returned the slave to the agent due to a defect that could arise later, either by evidence or by refusal of oath, then this return is to the principal. The agent does not need to contest with the principal. The proof: if the buyer established evidence that the defect existed at the time of the seller's possession, and the judge ordered the return based on that evidence, then the return is upon the principal because evidence is a complete proof. And in the case of the agent's refusal of the oath, the return is to the principal because the agent is compelled to refuse the oath due to his lack of familiarity with the slave's condition. Therefore, the responsibility reverts to the principal.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the return of a defective good in an agency context. When a buyer returns a defective item to the agent (wakil), and the defect is of a type that could have arisen after the sale (yuhdathu mithluh), the return must be accompanied by either evidence (bayyinah) proving the defect existed before the sale, or the agent's refusal to take an oath (nukul). The text explains that bayyinah is a complete proof (hujja mutlaqa) and therefore establishes the defect against the principal (muwakkil). Similarly, when the agent refuses the oath, it is because he lacks knowledge of the item's history, so he is compelled to avoid false swearing; hence the liability is placed on the principal who appointed him. In both cases, the agent need not engage in litigation (khusumat) with the principal to effect the return; the good is deemed returned to the principal directly by the court's decision. The reasoning is that the agent's ignorance is due to the principal's own doing in not providing him with full knowledge, so the principal must bear the consequence.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Clear evidence, typically two male witnesses, that proves a claim in court.
نكول (nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take a required oath, which results in the plaintiff's claim being upheld.
وكيل (wakil): The agent appointed by the principal to conduct a sale on his behalf.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
عيبيحدث مثله (ayb yahdathu mithluh): A defect that could possibly arise after the sale, such as an illness or injury that can occur over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a buyer returns a defective slave to the agent, and the defect is of a type that could have arisen after the sale, what is required for the return to be validly attributed to the principal?
A) The agent must confess to the defect.
B) The buyer must provide evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed before sale, or the agent must refuse the oath (nukul).
C) The principal must authorize the return.
D) The judge must personally inspect the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the return of a defective good to an agent binds the principal if supported by evidence (bayyinah) or the agent's refusal of oath (nukul), because both indicate the defect existed before the sale.

Agent's Confession and Liability for Defective Goods
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's confession liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an agent's confession of a defect binds the agent personally versus the principal, and state the agent's right to contest the principal under the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Agent's confession is a limited proof that binds himself, not the principal, but the agent can later contest the principal.

When an agent (wakil) sells an item and later confesses that the item was defective at the time of sale, a critical question arises: does this confession bind the agent or the principal? The Hanafi school rules that confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira) that primarily affects the confessor. However, the agent is given the right to seek recourse from the principal by providing evidence. This lesson unpacks the nuances, including an intra-Hanafi dispute about defects that cannot arise after the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage deals with a wakil (agent) who confesses (iqrar) to a defect in the sold item after the buyer returns it. The confession is the basis for the return.
The return can be with or without a court order (qada').
2
Apply the principle of hujja qasira
Confession is a limited proof that binds only the confessor. Since the agent was not compelled to confess (he could have remained silent or refused the oath), his confession binds him personally, not the principal.
However, the agent can later sue the principal using evidence (bayyinah) or the principal's refusal of oath (nukul).
3
Distinguish based on defect type
Two scenarios: (1) Defect can arise later (yuhdathu mithluh) – here, if the return is without a court order, the agent cannot contest the principal because it is considered a new sale to a third party. (2) Defect cannot arise later (la yahdathu mithluh) – a different ruling applies.
This distinction affects whether the return is considered 'determined' (mut'ayyan) or not.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For defects that cannot arise later, there is a difference of riwaya: In one narration, the confession binds the principal directly without the agent needing to contest. In the general narrations, the agent cannot contest the principal (so the principal is bound directly).
The text sides with the general narrations, noting that the right is in the description of freedom, then moves to return, not determined.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi position is that the agent is bound by his confession but retains the right to contest the principal with evidence or nukul. The agent's initial liability is personal.
The intra-Hanafi variation only surfaces in the specific case of a non-recurring defect without a court order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فان كان ذالك باقرار لزم المامور لان الاقرار حجة قاصرة وهو غير مضطر اليه لا مكانه السكوت والنكول الا ان له ان يخاصم المؤكل فيلزمه ببينة أو بنكوله بخلاف ما اذا كان الرد بغير قضاء باقرار والعيب يحدث مثله حيث لا يكون له أن يخاصم بائعه لانه بيع جديد فى حق ثالث والبائع ثالثهما والرد بالقضاء نسخ لعموم ولاية القاضى غير ان الحجة قاصرة وهي الاقرار فمن حيث الفسخ كان له ان يخاصمه ومن حيث القصور في الحجة لا يلزم المؤكل الابحجة ولو كان العيب لا يحدث مثله والرد بغير قضاء باقراره يلزم المؤكل من غير خصومة في رواية لان الرد متعين وفى عامة الروايات ليس له ان يخاصمه لما ذكرنا والحق في وصف السلام ثم ينتقل الى الرد ثم الى الرجوع بالنقصان فلم يتعين الرد وقد بيناه في الكفاية باطول من هذا. ترجمہ: پس اگر یہ واپسی وکیل کے اقرار سے ہو تو غلام وکیل پر لازم ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ اقرار حجت قاصرہ ہے اور وکیل کرنے میں مجبور بھی نہیں تھا۔ کیونکہ اس کیلئے خاموشی اختیار کرنا اور قسم سے انکار کرنا ممکن تھا۔ مگر وکیل کیلئے اس کی اجازت ہے کہ وہ موکل سے مخاصمہ کرے پھر غلام کو بینہ کے ذریعہ یا اس کے انکار عن الیمین کے ذریعہ اس پر لازم کر دے۔ برخلاف اس صورت کے جب واپسی وکیل کے اقرار سے بغیر قضاء کے ہو اور عیب ایسا ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ یہ تیسرے کے حق میں بیع جدید ہے اور بائع ان کا تیسرا ہے اور قضائے قاضی کی وجہ سے
He said: If that was by confession, it is binding on the agent (mamur) because confession is a limited proof, and he was not compelled to make it, as he could have remained silent or refused the oath. However, he has the right to contest the principal and make the latter liable by evidence or by the latter's refusal of oath. Unlike the case where the return is without a court order by confession and the defect is of a type that could arise later, where the agent cannot contest the principal because it is a new sale in the right of a third party, and the seller is a third party to them. The return by court order annuls the generality of the judge's authority, but the proof is limited and it is the confession. From the aspect of annulment, he can contest him, and from the aspect of the limitation of the proof, the principal is not bound except by proof. And if the defect is not of a type that could arise later, and the return is without a court order by his confession, it binds the principal without contest in one narration because the return is determined. In the general narrations, he cannot contest him for the reasons we mentioned. The right is in the description of freedom, then it moves to the return, then to recourse for diminution, so the return is not determined. We have explained this extensively in al-Kifaya. Translation: So if this return is by the agent's confession, the slave becomes binding on the agent. Because confession is a limited proof and he was not compelled to make it; he could have remained silent or refused the oath. But the agent is permitted to contest the principal and make the slave binding on him by evidence or by the principal's refusal of oath. Unlike the case where the return is without a court order by confession and the defect is such, because it is a new sale in the right of a third party, and the seller is their third party, and due to the judge's decision.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where the agent (wakil) personally confesses (iqrar) that the returned slave was defective at the time of sale. The ruling is that such a confession binds the agent himself (mamur), not the principal, because confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira) that only affects the confessor. However, the agent is given the right to contest (khusumat) the principal—that is, he can later sue the principal to recover the loss by providing evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed before delivery, or by having the principal refuse the oath (nukul). The passage then contrasts this with two other scenarios: (1) If the defect is of a type that can arise later (yuhdathu mithluh) and the return is without a court order (bi-ghayr qada') but by confession, then the agent cannot contest the principal because the transaction is considered a new sale regarding a third party (the original seller). (2) If the defect is not of a type that can arise later (la yahdathu mithluh) and the return is without a court order by confession, then according to one riwaya, the return binds the principal directly without the agent needing to contest; but in the majority of riwayat, the agent cannot contest the principal (meaning the binding is on the principal directly). The text notes that the different scenarios stem from whether the return is determined (mut'ayyan) or not. This reflects an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the agent's right to seek recourse when the defect is not of a recurring type.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Confession or admission of a fact, such as the existence of a defect in the sold item.
مامور (mamur): The agent who is ordered to sell; in this context, the wakil.
حجة قاصرة (hujja qasira): A proof that is limited in effect, binding only on the person who produces it or is subject to it.
رد بالاقرار (radd bi al-iqrar): Return of the sold item based on the agent's confession of a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the agent (wakil) returns a defective slave to the buyer by confessing (iqrar) the defect, who is initially bound by the return?
A) The principal (muwakkil)
B) The agent himself (mamur)
C) The judge who ordered the return
D) The buyer

Key Takeaway
An agent's confession of a defect binds him personally due to the limited nature of confession (hujja qasira), but he retains the right to shift liability to the principal with evidence or the principal's refusal of oath.

6 min
Return of Defective Goods by Agent: Evidence and Refusal to Swear Oath
Explaining that return due to evidence or refusal to oath binds the principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that when an agent returns a defective item based on conclusive evidence (bayyinah) or refusal to swear (nukul), the return is binding on the principal, and explain the reasoning based on istihsan.

Core Idea
Agent's return via bayyinah or nukul binds the principal due to the agent's compulsion.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah clarifies the agency relationship when a buyer returns a defective item. The ruling distinguishes between the agent's personal liability and the principal's responsibility when the return is based on court evidence or the agent's inability to swear an oath. Understanding this helps you apply the Hanafi principle that the principal bears the consequences of the agent's necessary actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the core legal outcome: the agent's return based on bayyinah or nukul binds the principal (muwakkil). This is a wajib ruling.
The binding is automatic; the agent does not need to litigate.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Recognize that the ruling covers two distinct cases: (1) return ordered by a judge after the buyer presents conclusive evidence (bayyinah), and (2) return resulting from the agent's refusal to take an oath (nukul).
Both scenarios lead to the same legal conclusion.
3
Analyze the proof for bayyinah
Understand that bayyinah is a complete proof (hujja kamila). When the buyer proves the defect existed at the time of sale, the defect is 'established' in the principal's possession, so the return is to the principal.
The evidence is conclusive; no further litigation with the principal is needed.
4
Analyze the proof for nukul
The agent is compelled (mudtar) to refuse the oath because he lacks familiarity with the item. His refusal is not an admission but a necessary consequence. Since the principal appointed him, the principal must bear the liability.
This is an istihsan reasoning: necessity overrides the general rule of personal liability.
5
Note the practical consequence
The agent does not need to initiate a lawsuit against the principal to enforce the return; the return is automatically effective against the principal.
This facilitates commerce and protects agents from undue hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
۲۶۴
اشرف الہدایہ جلد دہم
قسم کی وجہ سے۔ اسلئے کہ بینہ حجت کاملہ ہے۔ اور وکیل قسم سے انکار کرنے میں مجبور ہے کیونکہ مبیع کے ساتھ عدم ممارست کی وجہ سے عیب وکیل کے علم سے دور ہے۔ لہذا غلام مؤکل پر لازم ہوگا۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے وکیل کی طرف غلام کو ایسے عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کیا جس کے مثل پیدا ہو سکتا ہے اور غلام واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ بینہ کی وجہ سے ہوا ہو۔ یا وکیل کے قسم سے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے ہوا ہو ۔ دونوں صورتوں میں یہی واپسی مؤکل کی طرف ہوگی۔ مؤکل کی طرف واپس کرنے کیلئے وکیل خصومت کا محتاج نہ ہوگا۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر مشتری نے اس بات پر مبینہ قائم کیا کہ یہ عیب بائع کے قبضہ کے وقت بھی موجود تھا۔ اور قاضی نے اس بینہ کی وجہ سے غلام واپس کرنے کا حکم دیا تو یہ واپسی موکل پر ہو گی۔ اس لئے کہ بینہ حجت کا ملہ ہوتا ہے۔ پس بینہ کی وجہ سے عیب کا قیام مؤکل کے پاس ثابت ہو جائے گا۔ اور جب عیب کا قیام مؤکل کے پاس ثابت ہو گیا تو غلام کی واپسی بھی مؤکل ہی کی طرف ہوگی اور وکیل کے انکار عن الیمین کرنے کی صورت میں واپسی مؤکل کی طرف اسلئے ہوگی کہ وکیل قسم سے انکار کرنے پر مجبور ہے اور مجبور اسلئے ہے کہ اس وکیل کو مبیع یعنی غلام کے احوال سے زیادہ سابقہ نہیں پڑا ہے۔ لہذا اس کے عیوب سے بھی زیادہ واقف نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب وکیل غلام کے عیوب سے زیادہ واقف نہیں ہے تو وہ جھوٹی قسم کھانے سے ڈرے گا اور اس کے نتیجہ میں وہ قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دے گا اور وکیل کو اس مصیبت میں موکل ہی نے ڈالا ہے۔ لہذا اس کو چھٹکارا دلانا بھی موکل پر وانجب ہوگا یعنی وکیل پر جو ذمہ داری عائد ہوگی وہ موکل کی طرف لوٹ جائے گی۔ پس انکار عن الیمین کا حکم موکل پر لازم ہوگا۔ یعنی انکار عن الیمین کی وجہ سے غلام کی واپسی مؤکل پر ہوگی ۔ اور وکیل کو غلام واپس کرنے کیلئے مؤکل سے خصومت کرنے کی چنداں ضرورت نہ ہوگی۔
Book of Agency. Page 264, Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume 10. ... Because the evidence is a complete proof. And the agent is compelled to refuse the oath because due to lack of familiarity with the sold item, the defect is far from the agent's knowledge. Therefore, the slave will be binding on the principal. Explanation of the case: If the buyer returns the slave to the agent due to a defect that could appear anew, and the return of the slave occurred due to evidence, or due to the agent's refusal to take an oath, in both cases this return is to the principal. The agent does not need to litigate to return to the principal. The proof is that if the buyer established evidence that this defect existed at the time of the seller's possession, and the judge ordered the return of the slave due to this evidence, then this return is upon the principal. Because evidence is a complete proof. Thus, the existence of the defect is proven in the hands of the principal. And when the existence of the defect is proven in the hands of the principal, the return of the slave is also to the principal. In the case of the agent's refusal to take an oath, the return is to the principal because the agent is compelled to refuse the oath. He is compelled because the agent has not had much prior dealing with the slave, so he is not very familiar with its defects. When the agent is not very familiar with the defects of the slave, he will fear taking a false oath, and as a result he will refuse to take the oath. And the agent is placed in this difficulty by the principal, so it is also necessary for the principal to relieve him, meaning the liability that falls on the agent will revert to the principal. Therefore, the ruling of refusal to take an oath is binding on the principal. That is, due to refusal to take an oath, the return of the slave is upon the principal. And the agent does not need to litigate with the principal in order to return the slave.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the scenario where a buyer returns a defective slave to the agent who sold it on behalf of a principal. The key ruling is that if the return is based on conclusive evidence (bayyinah) or the agent's refusal to take an oath (nukul), the return is binding on the principal, not the agent. The reason is that the agent is not in a position to know the defect personally due to lack of familiarity with the merchandise, so the court's decision or the agent's inability to swear truthfully does not reflect personal fault. The evidence is a complete proof (hujja kamila) that establishes the defect existed at the time of sale, making the principal liable. Similarly, the agent's refusal to swear is not a voluntary admission but a consequence of being compelled by circumstances, which the principal caused by appointing him. Therefore, the agent need not litigate with the principal; the return is automatically effective against the principal. This ruling follows the Hanafi principle that the agent's actions in necessary situations bind the principal.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Conclusive evidence or proof presented in court.
نكول (nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath when required, leading to judgment against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a buyer returns a defective slave to an agent based on the agent's refusal to swear an oath (nukul), who is ultimately liable for the return?
A) The agent personally
B) The principal
C) The buyer
D) The judge

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent's return of a defective good based on conclusive evidence (bayyinah) or refusal to swear (nukul) binds the principal due to the agent's compulsion and the completeness of evidence.

Agent's Confession and Permanent Defects: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's confession for permanent defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi narrations regarding the binding effect of an agent's confession for permanent defects and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent's confession binds only him; for permanent defects, the general narrations require litigation before shifting liability.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: if an agent confesses a defect that cannot appear anew and the buyer returns the goods without a court order, does the return immediately bind the principal? One narration says yes; the general narrations say no. The resolution hinges on the nature of the buyer's rights and the limited effect of confession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal act
Determine the factual situation: an agent confesses a defect in a sold slave, the buyer returns the slave based solely on that confession without a court order, and the defect is permanent (cannot arise anew). The key question is whether the return binds the principal or only the agent, and whether the agent can later litigate with the principal.
Permanent defect: 'al-ayb la yahduthu mithluhu'.
2
State the two conflicting narrations
One narration (riwaya) holds that the return binds the principal immediately without litigation because the return is 'determined' (mut'ayyan). The general narrations (ammat al-riwayat) hold that the agent cannot litigate with the principal and the return does not bind the principal automatically.
The general narrations are the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each narration
First narration: since the defect cannot appear anew, the return is certain, so it binds the principal. General narrations: the buyer's right is first to claim the description of soundness, then to return, then to claim compensation for the decrease; thus the return is not determined. Confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira) binding only the confessor, so the agent must prove the defect existed with the principal.
The buyer's rights are sequential: soundness → return → compensation.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle of hujja qasira
Confession is a limited proof: it only binds the one who makes it. Therefore, the agent's confession makes the return binding on him personally, not on the principal. The principal is only bound if the agent provides evidence (bayyinah) that the defect existed with the principal, or if the principal refuses to take an oath (nukul).
Hujja qasira contrasts with bayyinah (complete proof) which binds both parties.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the practical consequence
The general narrations are the mufta bihi. Thus, the agent must litigate with the principal; the principal is not immediately liable. The agent remains personally bound unless he proves the defect existed with the principal. This protects the principal from being bound by the agent's unauthorized admission.
The author references al-Kifayah for a fuller explanation, indicating reliance on the general narrations.

He said: If that (return) occurs by confession (iqrar), it binds the agent (al-ma'mur), because confession is a limited proof (hujja qasira), and he is not compelled to it, because silence and refusal to swear were possible for him. However, he has the right to litigate with the principal and make it binding upon him by evidence or by his refusal. Unlike the case where the return is without a court order by confession and the defect is of a type that can appear anew, where he has no right to litigate with his seller, because it is a new sale regarding a third party, and the seller is the third of them. And the return by court order is a rescission due to the general authority of the judge, except that the proof is limited, which is the confession. So in terms of rescission, he can litigate with him, but due to the limitation of the proof, it is not binding on the principal except by evidence. And if the defect is of a type that cannot appear anew, and the return is without court order by his confession, in one narration it binds the principal without litigation because the return is determined. But in the general narrations, he has no right to litigate with him due to what we mentioned. And the right is in the description of soundness, then it transfers to return, then to recourse for the decrease, so the return is not determined. And we have explained this at greater length in al-Kifayah. Translation: So if this return is by the agent's confession, the slave will be binding on the agent. Because confession is a limited proof, and he was not compelled to make it. Because it was possible for him to remain silent or refuse the oath. But the agent is permitted to litigate with the principal, then make the slave binding upon him by evidence or by his refusal. Contrary to the case when the return is by confession without a court order and the defect is such that it can appear anew. Because this is a new sale regarding a third party, and the seller is the third of them. And due to the judge's order, the return is a rescission because of the judge's general authority. However, the proof is limited, i.e., confession. So due to being a rescission, the agent is allowed to litigate with the principal, and due to the limitation of the proof, this return is not binding on the principal except by evidence. And if the defect is of a type that cannot appear anew, and the return is by confession without a court order, then in one narration it binds the principal without litigation, because the return is determined. And in the majority of narrations, he has no right to litigate with him due to the reason we mentioned. And the right is in the description of soundness, then it transfers to return, then to recourse for the decrease, so the return is not determined. And we have explained this in al-Kifayah at greater length. Explanation of the case: If the agent confessed to a defect in the purchased slave, and due to this confession the buyer returned the defective slave to the agent by the judge's decision, then this slave will be binding on the agent. That is, this return will be counted against the agent, not against the principal. The proof is that confession is a limited proof. Therefore, its effect appears only in favor of the one who made the confession, not any other. That is, due to the confession, the return of the slave is to the one who confessed (the agent), not to the principal. And the agent was not compelled to confess because he could have remained silent or refused the oath. So if he had not confessed but remained silent or refused to take the oath when demanded, the judgment would have been based on silence or refusal, not on confession. In any case, the agent was not compelled to confess the defect; he was free. And since he was not compelled, his confession only affects him. So the return of the defective slave is to him, and his confession does not affect the principal. That is, this return is not binding on the principal. However, the agent has full right to litigate with his principal. Namely, the agent will bring a claim before the court and present evidence that this defect also existed with the principal. If the agent provides evidence, then this slave will be binding on the principal. That is, the agent will return the slave to the principal. And if the agent cannot provide evidence, then the principal will be asked to take an oath. If the principal refuses to take an oath, then the slave will also be returned to the principal. Contrary to the case where the agent confessed the defect and the slave was returned to the agent purely due to his confession without a court order, and the defect is of a type that can appear anew. In this case, the agent does not have the right to litigate with his seller, i.e., his principal. Because the return of the slave to the agent due solely to the agent's confession without a court order, although it is a rescission between the agent and the buyer, it is a new sale regarding a third party. That is, from the principal's perspective, it is as if the buyer sold this slave to the agent. So the agent has no right to litigate with his principal regarding this slave. And returning the defective slave to the agent by court order is a rescission. Because the judge has general authority. But this is a rescission based on a limited proof, i.e., confession. So because the proof is limited, returning the defective slave to the agent does not mean returning him to the principal. That is, returning him to the agent does not make him return to the principal, unless the agent provides evidence or the principal refuses to take an oath. And since this return is a rescission, the agent has full right to litigate with his principal because in the case of rescission of a sale, the agent is authorized to litigate with his principal. And if a defect is found in the slave that cannot appear anew, and the buyer returned the slave to the agent purely due to the agent's confession without a court order, then...
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with the legal consequences when an agent confesses to a defect in the sold item, leading to its return by the buyer. The central principle is that an agent's confession is a 'hujja qasira' (limited proof) that only binds the confessor and not others. Thus, if the return is based solely on the agent's confession, the agent is personally liable for the defective slave, not the principal. However, the agent is permitted to litigate with the principal to shift the liability back, provided he can produce evidence (bayyinah) or the principal refuses to take an oath (nukul). The text distinguishes between two scenarios: (a) when the defect is of a type that could appear anew (yahduthu mithluhu) and the return is without a court order, the return is considered a new sale between the buyer and the agent, so the agent cannot litigate with the principal. (b) When the defect is permanent (la yahduthu mithluhu), there is a dispute within the Hanafi school: in one narration (riwaya), the return binds the principal immediately without litigation because the return is 'determined'; but in the general narrations, the agent cannot litigate. The text explains that the buyer's right is first the description of soundness, then return, then recourse for decrease, so return is not determined. This clarifies that the agent's confession alone does not automatically bind the principal unless supported by stronger evidence. The author references al-Kifayah for a fuller discussion.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Voluntary confession or admission of a fact, here the agent's admission of a defect.
حجة قاصرة (hujja qasira): A proof that only affects the one who produces it, such as a confession.
نسخ (naskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, here the return of a defective item cancels the sale.
عيب لا يحدث مثله (ayb la yahduthu mithluhu): A defect that cannot appear anew after the sale, i.e., a permanent defect that must have existed at the time of sale.
عامة الروايات (ammat al-riwayat): The majority or general narrations from the Hanafi masters, as opposed to a single or weak narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, when an agent confesses a permanent defect and the buyer returns the goods without a court order, what is the status of the return regarding the principal?
A) The return immediately binds the principal without litigation.
B) The return binds the agent, but he may litigate with the principal to shift liability.
C) The return binds the agent, and he cannot litigate with the principal; he must prove the defect existed with the principal.
D) The return is void because the defect is permanent.

Key Takeaway
An agent's confession of a permanent defect binds only the agent; the principal is not liable until the agent proves the defect existed with him, following the general narrations of the Hanafi school.

General Ruling: Agent's Acknowledgment Binds the Agent, Not the Principal
Ruling on general effect of wakil's iqrar on radd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an agent's acknowledgment (iqrar) of a defect in a sold item binds only the agent, and identify the conditions under which the agent can litigate with the principal.

Core Idea
Iqrar is hujjat qasirah: it binds only the one who acknowledges, not third parties.

This lesson examines the general Hanafi ruling on the effect of an agent's acknowledgment of a defect when the buyer returns the defective slave. The key principle is that iqrar is a limited proof, so the return affects only the agent. However, the agent can litigate with the principal if a qadi orders the return. We will also see a specific scenario where the agent cannot litigate because a new sale is deemed to have occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the agent's (wakil) acknowledgment of a defect in a sold slave, and the buyer's return (radd) of the slave to the agent. Determine the legal effect of this return on the agent and the principal (muwakkil).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the principle that iqrar is a hujjat qasirah (limited proof). Understand how this principle is derived from the general rules of evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the limited nature of iqrar leads to the ruling: the return is effective against the agent only. The agent's acknowledgment cannot bind the principal because the principal did not acknowledge.
This is the core reasoning: the proof's effect is confined to the person who produced it.
4
Apply the ruling to two scenarios
First scenario: the qadi orders the return based on the agent's acknowledgment. The slave returns to the agent, but the agent can litigate with the principal. Second scenario: the defect is replicable and the buyer returns without qadi's judgment. The return is considered a new sale between agent and buyer, so the agent cannot litigate with the principal.
In the second scenario, the transaction is treated as a new sale (bai' jadid) between agent and buyer concerning third parties.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of this ruling: under the general view, the agent must litigate to recover the price from the principal. If the agent cannot litigate (second scenario), he bears the loss himself.
